# 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_155 messages · 34 participants · 2001-06 → 2001-07_

---

### 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

- **From:** klatteross@aol.commmm (Ross Klatte)
- **Date:** 2001-06-12T23:09:11+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com>`

```
1.  JAVA can read a string as an integer with the elegant
    int iVar=Integer.valueOf(stringVar).valueInt;
    cobol has the boring
    03 xVar                pic XXX.
    03 iVar redefines xVar pic 999.
B-o-r-i-n-g.  

2.  JAVA has a wide assortment of methods to read input data--InputStream,
    FileInputStream, FilterInputStream, readChar, readChars--just to name
    a tiny handful.  
    cobol has nothing but one miserable little READ verb.  
B-o-rrr-inggg. 

3.  JAVA reads data the way it is supposed to be read--one byte at a time. 
    cobol lumps everything into one monolithic monstrosity called a "record." 
Boring.  Boring and primitive.  

    
Ross
http://www.geocities.com/ross_klatte/
```

Ross Klatte wrote:

Of course you are being deliberately provocative - but BS anyway ! (loved your
tongue in cheek abut Brits abusing the American language <G>).

Now math is not my great skill, but 1, 2 and 3 - where are the other seven ? To
save unnecessary repetition - ever heard of OO COBOL - OK your current compiler
doesn't have it, it is not as yet an 'official' part of the standard but is
available from Fujitsu, Merant(Net Express), IBM (Visual Age) and if you wanna
take a trip, Hitachi - but they only currently sell to the Japanese market. And
believe me pardner - it WORKS! FAST !

And regarding fast - I continuously read about the JVM and its performance. You
gotta make an executable to make it compare to COBOL.

Don't know about you buster - I like things in Innglish - you know the Brit type.
To me your so called 'elegant' Java is absolute gibberish. I'd be dabbling around
the British Museum studying Egyptology if I was enthralled about cryptography.
Plain English - plain communication.

>
> 2.  JAVA has a wide assortment of methods to read input data--InputStream,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I don't use - but since DOS days Micro Focus handled byte stream files. How many
ways do you want to read, reminds me of "Fifty ways to leave your lover".
As you say Java, many ways - what you didn't mention, does it have the self
contained feature read PrimeKey or Alternate Key or Split-Key.

I can't think of an occasion when I'd want to readChar (one byte). Are we
comparing apples with apples - in COBOL we are talking about real world business
applications.

If you MUST HAVE your various read methods, absolutely nothing to stop you
writing COBOL methods to do that.

> 3.  JAVA reads data the way it is supposed to be read--one byte at a time.
>     cobol lumps everything into one monolithic monstrosity called a "record."
> Boring.  Boring and primitive.
>

"Supposed to be read" - who said so. Where's your argument the the following can
be addressed more efficiently in Java :-

   01 Readings-record.
        05 PrimeKey etc...
        05 occurs 10.
            10 Reading occurs 24 pic 9(02)v9(03),

Read record using PrimeKey - whammo - I've got it all back in one instruction. OK
adjust that to rows using DB and ESQL - but parallel result.

4. Don't like some of the verbosity associated with OO COBOL. Net Express has the
class Vocabulary, kinda functions like the copyfile :-

        replace ==(tag)== by ==xyz==.

If I *really* disliked using :-

    invoke ThisObject "get-abc" returning thisthing  *>(either text or object)

Having defined the vocab 'exchange' I could substitute :-

    makeACall ThisObject "get-abc" returning thisthing *> (either text or object)

Compiler does a look-up and substitutes word 'invoke'.

Agreed - COBOL and Java DO compliment one another and certainly some are using
Java to front-end COBOL business logic with Java GUIs. (You shouldn't overlook
the current complaints by Java developers - it has unnecessary bloat to handle
GUI-ng). I'm not in the least anti Java - I just LIKE COBOL and it does the job !

Ross, with a surname like 'Klatte' you must be thinking Double Dutch. Get outta
here - it aint April 01 yet !

Jimmy, Calgary AB

#### ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>

Very droll...

Shame there are 7 reasons missing...

Here they are:

4. Java is innately Object Oriented and requires no Repository.

5. Java extends Classes in a simple logical way. (although it can only
inherit from ONE parent Class at a time; unlike OO COBOL which supports
multiple inheritance. Funny, I've never needed multiple inheritance and NOT
having it simplifies the whole OO inheritance model. Guess If I did need it
I'd use C++....)

6. Java is less verbose than COBOL and much quicker to write. It is also
easier to test and debug (mainly because there is much less of it...) and
requires NO Run Time fees to distribute.

7. Java runs on ANYTHING including the washing machine and the toaster...(if
you have a smart toaster).

8. Java is not held back by a moribund Standards committee who will take 10
years to pontificate over what should be in it.

9. People who use Java can actually participate in the development of the
language on-line and directly to Sun's site.  Java Programmers can even
contribute Classes for inclusion with the next release of the Language (and
they do...)

10. Java can be used to build components (Beans) which will run on Clients,
Servers, or the Web. The software development kit to do this is FREE and SO
is the Run Time. (cf with COBOL...)

I could go on...<G>

more below...


"Ross Klatte" <klatteross@aol.commmm> wrote in message
news:20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com...

>>>
> 1.  JAVA can read a string as an integer with the elegant
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
<<<
Unfair comparison! The COBOL code which purports to be equivalent simply
defines the data. The Java code actually loads iVar...
In both cases the resulting data conversions are transparent to the
Programmer.

I could also achieve the above with the following Java statement:

int iVar = string2num.convert(String)

The true COBOL equivalent would be:

invoke string2num "Convert" using String
                                                            iVar
end-invoke

...where "string2num" is a Component I wrote which does a lot more than the
simple numeric conversion effected by the COBOL MOVE...

The point is, are the above two examples REALLY so different?

>>>
> 2.  JAVA has a wide assortment of methods to read input data--InputStream,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> B-o-rrr-inggg.
<<<
Are you suggesting that having a "wide assortment of methods to read input
data" is actually a DISADVANTAGE for a computer programming language? A
curious take on it...

>>>
> 3.  JAVA reads data the way it is supposed to be read--one byte at a time.
>     cobol lumps everything into one monolithic monstrosity called a
"record."
> Boring.  Boring and primitive.
>
<<<
COBOL also supports Byte Stream input (at least Merant and Fujitsu  versions
do...I'm not getting into a discussion over "valid COBOL...<G>)

The File Stream I/O in Java is a Programmer choice and, if you use it, it
can be used to load "structures" ("records" in COBOL speak...).

While I realise your post was tongue  in cheek, Ross, there are many
"threatened" COBOL programmers who will see denigration of the perceived
"opposition" as a more comfortable path than actually sitting down and
expanding their skill set by learning Java.

I have never, and do not currently, advocate Java as a REPLACEMENT for
COBOL. It is a COMPLEMENT to COBOL. BOTH of these languages will be required
in the toolbox of the future.
(I have covered all this at length elsewhere so I won't go into it again
here.)

I strongly recommend COBOL programmers to get into Java, mainly for the
following reasons:

1. The marketplace of tomorrow will not support the "One Language Wonder"
mentality. ("I can do everything I want to do in COBOL..."). If you intend
to be a Professional Programmer you will need to have the best tools for the
job. COBOL is the best tool for solving commercial business data processing
problems; it is NOT the best tool for enabling certain other aspects of the
new technology.

2. New methods and approaches to commercial business data processing may
render even COBOL unnecessary (except maybe "behind the scenes"). Component
based technology will enable non-technical Business Users who never
programmed anything in their lives, to quickly and easily build commercial
systems using just iteration, interaction, and Business knowledge.

3. Java is a "Fast Path" to understanding Object Orientation. OO is going to
figure large in everybody's future, whether you like it or not... Pick up
this knowledge now, and the quickest way to do it is to learn Java. You can
then easily extend the newly acquired concepts into OO COBOL AS WELL as
Java.

4. Like COBOL, Java is fun to use and it looks good on a CV, costs you
nothing (apart from time and effort - and not too much of that...<G>), and
it provides insight into another way of programming from the one you are
used to.

There should NOT be a CONTEST between Java and COBOL. It's like the saw
insulting the hammer because it doesn't know how to cut... When you need to
nail something it takes much longer hitting it with a saw than if you have a
hammer handy...

I love COBOL and have done for 35 years. However, it is not a blind
love...<G> My experience is richer for having acquired Java skill.

If anyone reading this is persuaded, even slightly, by the above, then I
would recommend the following starting points:

1. "Java Made Simple" by Peter McBride (ISBN 0-7506-3241-0)

A very good introduction to the basic concepts (including OO) of Java.

2. "SAMS Teach Yourself Java in 24 Hours" by Rogers Cadenhead (ISBN
0-672-31630-7)

This is sprinkled with a wicked sense of humour which makes the book
extremely entertaining as well as informative.

Either or both of the above wll give a COBOL progammer a really good
grounding  and appreciation of Java. Having covered them, you might then
move on to the 1000 page "Java2 Complete" which is really invaluable for
advanced OO programming.

"The BEST tools for the Job..." That's what professional programming and
Component building are all about...

I would be very happy to receive private mail with your experiences on using
the above, and will gladly help if you are stuck on any points.

Pete.

> P.S.  I have been studying Java for 4 months now, and I
> still don't understand the "this."  Any enlightening tips?
>
Well, "enlightenment" is a big ask, but let's take the first faltering steps
towards Nirvana <G>....

First off, you are reading the wrong books if it isn't clear after four
months... See my suggestions for reading elsewhere in this thread...

"this" in Java serves exactly the same purpose as "self" in OO COBOL.
("POW-SELF" in PowerCOBOL, which is intrinsically Object Oriented.)

It means "use the object we are currently in". Another way to look at it is
"pass this object as the subject of the Method I am invoking".

Here's a Java example:

Java uses the delegation model to handle Event processing. Events are passed
from source controls to LISTENER objects. That means you will connect a
"listener" to the control that raises the event (e.g. a button click). When
an event occurs, the "Listener" object will "hear" it.  You do this by
invoking the "addActionListener()" method. This method belongs to the
control which raises the event (in this case, a button). But what if the
object you want to be the "Listener" is the actual Applet you are writing ?
You will not necessarily have an Object Reference to the current object (in
this case the Applet).

So, you use "this", which is like an implicit Object Reference to the
current object.

Having written the Java code to add the button to your Applet, you then
invoke the addActionListener method and pass "this" as the reference. Like
this...

public void init()
    ...
    button1 = new Button("Click Me!");
    add(button1);
    button1.addActionListener(this);
    ...
From now on whenever button1 is clicked, your Applet will be notified. How
you actually action the event or tie it back to whichever control raised it,
is beyond the scope of this post, but it involves overriding the
ActionListener interface's ActionPerformed method.

Here's an OO COBOL example, for comparison:

In OO COBOL we only really use "self" when we wish to specify a Method which
is LOWER (or equal, but then it is redundant) in the inheritance hierarchy.

consider 3 classes: ClassA, ClassB, and ClassC

ClassC inherits from ClassB which inherits from ClassA. (This is very useful
for combining "common" elements of a Class and then "extending" it to
accommodate specific extensions. ClassA would define the basic attributes,
ClassB would extend this, and ClassC would extend it even further.)

A concrete example?

ClassA: Pets
Attributes: species, name, colour
Methods: New, GetSpecies, GetName, GetColour, SetSpecies, SetName,
SetColour, FeedIt

ClassB (inherits ClassA): Haslegs
Attributes: numberLegs
Methods: Groom, GetNumberLegs, SetNumberLegs

ClassC (inherits ClassB): Dogs
Attributes: registration, breed
Methods: GetRegistration, SetRegistration, GetBreed, SetBreed, Roll Over,
Fetch, Walkies, Speak

Now let's say that Dogs which are NOT registered must not be groomed...

I am coding the "Groom" Method in ClassB (HasLegs).  I need to know if what
I'm grooming is a dog, and if it is, whether it is registered...

*> This looks UP the hierarchy...
invoke SUPER "GetSpecies"  returning local-species
end-invoke

evaluate local-species
    when  'dog'
*> This looks DOWN the hierarchy...
         invoke SELF "GetRegistration" returning local-reg
         end-invoke
         if NOT registered
            display "unregistered dogs cannot be groomed, sorry..."
            exit method
        else
           display "nice doggie...stroke stroke..."
        end-if
     when 'horse'
            display "curry comb... curry comb...easy boy..."
     when other
           display "stroke...stroke...don't bite me...stroke... stroke"
end-evaluate
exit method

Note that the HasLegs Class does NOT have EITHER the 'GetSpecies' or
'GetRegistration' Methods...they are provided by inheritance, and 'SUPER'
and 'SELF' control the "direction" of the inheritance search.

(the Java equivalents are 'SUPER' and 'THIS')

Another important use of SELF and SUPER is in cases where the inheritance
hierarchy has several methods  with the same name (quite possible with the
polymorphic nature of OO). It is necessary to "qualify" which one you want
(especially important if there is a like named method both above and
below...)

I am pleased to see you extending your skill base by learning Java.
Persevere with it; it is different to COBOL, and even OO COBOL. Try not to
be too quick to write off things which don't work the way you might
expect... I am learning more about both languages every day, and many of the
things I thought bizarre at first are now part of accepted practise...

There is a good base of OO expertise available through this NG and most
questions should get answered.

I hope the above has clarified "this" for you.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 5)_

- **From:** Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2001-06-15T12:05:20-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com>`

```
"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

> But it mystifies me that

> > a large number of reputedly level-headed businessmen have
> > gone along with that view.
>
> Why? There are proven cost benefits from the proper application of OO.

Each choice also has some increased costs as well.  Some times the benefits of
OO outweigh its costs - but it hasn't been demonstrated that this is the case in
an enterprise business environment.

Of course things change.  Right now, there is no way any enterprise I have been
involved with will accept changing a component without testing everything which
uses that component (with real live tests).   Since testing is a high cost item
with the trend for it to get higher, we don't find large enterprises putting
business rules into objects.   Things which don't matter so much, such as a GUI
button on a screen are a different matter.  Not much system testing is
required.  And if the system is small enough that one person can know it, the
testing is more manageable.

And not allowing one to modify components is not generally considered "the
proper application of OO".

An exception may come from a company building an integrated business environment
for sale.  If J.D. Edwards creates an OO product and sells it to a thousand
companies, then they can have a permanent staff doing rigorous testing of the
system.  System testing from adding an integrated component is no greater than
what is happening anyway for isolated components.  The benefits of integration
for a product which has to adjust to a lot of customers are easier to see and
measure.  In this environment, there really are no significant increased testing
costs.  So the cost/benefit ratio of going OO are different again.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Grinder" <no.spam@no.spam.spam.spam.net>
- **Date:** 2001-06-15T23:54:50-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kBBW6.12199$I6.259641@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net>`

```
> Each choice also has some increased costs as well.  Some times the
benefits of
> OO outweigh its costs - but it hasn't been demonstrated that this is the
case in
> an enterprise business environment.

I'm currently engaged in the upkeep of two applications.  One
is procedural Cobol, the other, Visual Basic.  On a weekly basis
I see the benefit of VB's OO-ish approach over the flat style of
the Cobol application.

If a bug is found, it is most often easy to find in the VB application.  A
strict organization of object responsibility allows for the likely bit to
code
immediately suggest itself.  This is a direct result of allowing code
to be organized by what it does, instead of when it does it.

Functions and procedures, methods and properties compartmentalize
code further--into short, specific chunks of logic.  It's easier to
comprehend
ten 20 line routines, than one 200 line program.  Indeed this comparison
is imbalanced, as it takes significantly less OO code than its inlined
counterpart.

For bug fixes and enhancements alike, code revisions are generally
centralized--you don't have to hunt through lots of source code to look
for a pattern to amend.  You can centralize Cobol code with copybooks,
but we've found that name collision limits this approach.

> Of course things change.  Right now, there is no way any enterprise I have
been
> involved with will accept changing a component without testing everything
which
> uses that component (with real live tests).

I agree that being able to do this is a luxury, but one that can be afforded
if you maintain a static (or at least expanding) interface to your objects,
you can regularily change internals without breaking suscribing
applications.

At a minimum, a growth organization should be driven to make bug fixes,
even if they're not engaged in development.  OO makes this easier.

>                                                               Since
testing is a high cost item
> with the trend for it to get higher, we don't find large enterprises
putting
> business rules into objects.

I think this is a goal worth striving for.  If you can distill your business
logic
into a concentrated set of code, with suitable interfaces, you have an edge.
You can keep your market response peppy, while allowing the infrastructural
components--like the GUI and data layers--to shake-out.
__________________

OO is more complex, but it's not a sophistication you escape with procedural
code--it just wanders into the relationships between your applications and
libraries.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-16T06:15:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B2AFA19.F3156790@home.com>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <kBBW6.12199$I6.259641@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com>`

```


Grinder wrote:

> > Each choice also has some increased costs as well.  Some times the
> benefits of
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Well Howard he sure makes a good case for OO and he's talking VB. He certainly
addressed your 'maintainability mantra'. Mr."Grinder", if you can get your hands
on an OO COBOL compiler - you should be ecstatic.

> You can centralize Cobol code with copybooks,
> but we've found that name collision limits this approach.
>

Within OO COBOL I find copybooks are an extremely  useful feature - but
'collision' doesn't occur because you can have your copybooks either 'Global
(W/S) or 'Localized' to a method :-

    Class :
    *>-----------------------
    Working-storage section.    *> "Global"
    copy 'mycopy.cpy' replacing ==(tag)== by ==WS==.
    *>-----------------------
    method-id. "mymethod".
    *>-----------------------
    Working-storage section.
    copy 'mycopy.cpy' replacing ==(tag)== by ==WS==.
    Local-storage section.
    copy 'mycopy.cpy' replacing ==(tag)== by ==LS==.
    Linkage section.
    copy 'mycopy.cpy' replacing ==(tag)== by ==IN==.
    copy 'mycopy.cpy' replacing ==(tag)== by ==OUT==.

    Procedure Division using IN-rec returning OUT-rec.

Note the 'Local' W/S entry currently available in Net Express will NOT be a part
of the new standard. The rest is.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```


Grinder wrote:

> > Each choice also has some increased costs as well.  Some times the
> benefits of
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the Cobol application.

It could be.   The advantages of OO are certainly more manifest in the types of
environments where a Visual Basic solution is possible, while the disadvantage
that I mentioned does not apply.  But why use an OO-ish approach when OO CoBOL
is available?    The only time I did extensive work in an OO-ish language it was
Paradox for windows.  It was the hardest thing to debug that I have ever worked
with, with each piece of code separate from the rest.  That separation had no
corresponding advantage that I could see (such as having a debugged component in
a library.


> If a bug is found, it is most often easy to find in the VB application.  A
> strict organization of object responsibility allows for the likely bit to
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> comprehend ten 20 line routines, than one 200 line program.

This argument applies just as well to structured CoBOL.  OO has other advantages
which count for more.


> Indeed this comparison is imbalanced, as it takes significantly less OO code
> than its inlined counterpart.

I have to ask people who use OO CoBOL - how true is this statement?


> For bug fixes and enhancements alike, code revisions are generally
> centralized--you don't have to hunt through lots of source code to look
> for a pattern to amend.  You can centralize Cobol code with copybooks,
> but we've found that name collision limits this approach.

I have seen and used the exact opposite argument.   For bug fixes and
enhancements, old type CoBOL code is centralized - except for copybooks, data
dictionaries, and calls everything is in one place - the program!  The more you
spread out the source, the less centralized it is, and the harder it is to hunt
through lots of source to look for the code which applies.


> > Of course things change.  Right now, there is no way any enterprise I have
> been
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> applications.

I don't think testing is a luxury.  Maybe that's why enterprises haven't
switched to OO, as my belief is more common in enterprise environments than
yours is.

> At a minimum, a growth organization should be driven to make bug fixes,
> even if they're not engaged in development.  OO makes this easier.

It could be.  But the bigger the system, the higher the testing component of
these bug fixes.

> OO is more complex, but it's not a sophistication you escape with procedural
> code--it just wanders into the relationships between your applications and
> libraries.

Which means, we have more dependencies.  In a typical enterprise shop, we have
lots of semi-duplicated code.   One customer wants almost the same report as
another customer, so we clone a program.   Keeping two similar programs is
costly, and the OO people are right to point to that cost.   But we don't need
to get the first customer to sign off on maintenance done to the second
customer's program.  If a business rule changes for one customer, we don't have
to figure out a generic way to make it different for that customer - we simply
change that customer's program.

As I keep saying - each choice we make has some costs and some benefits.   The
choice we make for one environment isn't always the same choice we would make in
a different environment.  And as costs change, environments change and we should
re-evaluate our decisions.
```

A colleague of mine who had half a dozen years in COBOL and
now has half a dozen years in Java emphasizes that
well-written and well-documented code in Any-language-X is
easier to maintain than badly-written and non-documented 
code in Any-language-Y.  But assuming the code is badly
written and undocumented (and if it were otherwise, why
would they be getting 200 per hour for him?), then Java is
more difficult to maintain than COBOL.  He says, "Everything's
scattered all over to hell and back."  

Is it easier to find a needle hidden in a haystack, or to find a 
needle hidden in one of ten thousand tablespoons filled with
hay?  If you can use a book of matches and a magnet, it
will be probably be quicker to find it in the haystack.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-16T20:15:49+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B2BBF3D.4936AC27@home.com>`
- **References:** `<3B2B5D24.D8F90EA9@brazee.net> <20010616103510.11216.00001731@ng-ce1.aol.com>`

```


Ross Klatte wrote:

> A colleague of mine who had half a dozen years in COBOL and
> now has half a dozen years in Java emphasizes that
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> will be probably be quicker to find it in the haystack.

Ross,

There really is no mystery to this. You and I can sit down and write a
program, Structured COBOL, OO COBOL, VB,  Java whatever. They are all
just tools. We can use them effectively or make a complete balls-up.

It has to start with DESIGN. What are the overall objectives of the
application and where does our particular 'piece' fit in. Because of
wishy-washy user input or poor EDP leadershp, ('IT" I believe is now the
politically correct term),  an application has the potential to be a
non-starter, even before somebody starts writing the first line of code.

Whether or not you are using non-OO or OO languages the 'problem' still
has to go through the same set of hoops, and the same analysis techniques
apply. Because of OO methodology, without question you have much more
flexibility in terms of reusable components (methods). But it gets back
to how is the design structured.
Part of that process of design for OO has to be, that you MUST come up
with a sensible hierarchy of classes, picking on logical groups of
actions, calling them super classes with variations suborned as
'sub-classes'.

If you don't figure out a logical structure for these units, which you
are constantly able to refer to (invoke), then agreed you finish up in a
maniacal haystack. (One of my regrets - don't see real haystacks around
any more - used to love sliding down them as a kid).

Does that imply I've figured it out. No sir !. Because the COBOL OO biz
is so new, without models to work from, there are several occasions where
I have back-tracked to arrive at a more meaningful and more easily
understood structure of classes. Don Tees who is using Fujitsu could also
confirm that he has done the same thing.

You can take a crack at OO COBOL in two ways, only use OO classes, or use
OO classes combined with 'Structureds'. Purely for consistency of coding
style I opted for the OO class approach. In my case it has paid off; the
style in effect allowing me a 'reusability' approach when viewing
disparate operations - if you like, different Business actions called
from a main menu.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:3B2B5D24.D8F90EA9@brazee.net...

> But why use an OO-ish approach when OO CoBOL is available?

Wouldn't using OO Cobol be an OO-ish approach?  I didn't call VB OO because
it does not support inheritance, a common OO feature.  OO-ish, though, is a
subset of OO, so OO would be OO-ish and more.

>  The only time I did extensive work in an OO-ish language it was
> Paradox for windows.  It was the hardest thing to debug that I have ever
worked
> with, with each piece of code separate from the rest.  That separation had
no
> corresponding advantage that I could see (such as having a debugged
component in
> a library.

Decent OO code doesn't spread out.  Under any methodology, it's good
form to limit copy-and-paste mentality.  It's just that I've found an OO
system more encouraging towards these ends.  If you think in terms of
encapsulation of function, the code should condense into related sets
of functions.  If the seperation of the code in the Paradox application
you worked with did not suggest an organization, it sounds like a bad
design--one that the developer would have worked against the system
to achieve.

> This argument applies just as well to structured CoBOL.  OO has other
advantages
> which count for more.

Structured cobol is method of simulating scope (and to a degree,
encapsulation)
in the code.  It's a good idea, but not one that has specific support from
the
language.  Conventional methodologies are always hard to live up to than
ones
mandated by the language.

> > Indeed this comparison is imbalanced, as it takes significantly less OO
code
> > than its inlined counterpart.
>
> I have to ask people who use OO CoBOL - how true is this statement?

Third party experience is of interest to me, but I've seen this work
already.
I've taken a couple of programs from my procedural code base and have
converted them to OO Cobol.  There's less than half of the original code.
To be fair, the original code had some dead wood in it.  Some related units
(yet untouched) will benefit, though, from the re-factored code.  I suspect
that a large scale attack on the applications would yield a signficant
concentration
of the code.

> > For bug fixes and enhancements alike, code revisions are generally
> > centralized--you don't have to hunt through lots of source code to look
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> enhancements, old type CoBOL code is centralized - except for copybooks,
data
> dictionaries, and calls everything is in one place - the program!  The
more you
> spread out the source, the less centralized it is, and the harder it is to
hunt
> through lots of source to look for the code which applies.

If you have an algorithm that's needed in multiple applications, calculation
of
tax for instance, how do you keep that in one place so that future edits can
adjust all users of that code?  Copybooks seem to be the only way.  If you
"centralize" it in each application, you commit yourself to multiple edits
in
the future.

> > > Of course things change.  Right now, there is no way any enterprise I
have
> > been
> > > involved with will accept changing a component without testing
everything
> > which
> > > uses that component (with real live tests).
> >
> > I agree that being able to do this is a luxury, but one that can be
afforded
> > if you maintain a static (or at least expanding) interface to your
objects,
> > you can regularily change internals without breaking suscribing
> > applications.
>
> I don't think testing is a luxury.  Maybe that's why enterprises haven't
> switched to OO, as my belief is more common in enterprise environments
than
> yours is.

I don't think testing is a luxury--it's being able to make discrete changes
to
the application without invalidating every related app, that I'm talking
about.
Does being able to do that seem reckless?

> > At a minimum, a growth organization should be driven to make bug fixes,
> > even if they're not engaged in development.  OO makes this easier.
>
> It could be.  But the bigger the system, the higher the testing component
of
> these bug fixes.

This is right in the area of my point.  If you have a strongly encapsulated
set
of code, you can limit the impact on the entire application when you make
changes to individual components.

> > OO is more complex, but it's not a sophistication you escape with
procedural
> > code--it just wanders into the relationships between your applications
and
> > libraries.
>
> Which means, we have more dependencies.

For a given set of specifications for application, I believe there are the
same
number of relationships/dependencies.  If you code carefully, in any
language,
you can limit the amount of parallel implementations of these relationships.

>                                                             In a typical
enterprise shop, we have
> lots of semi-duplicated code.   One customer wants almost the same report
as
> another customer, so we clone a program.   Keeping two similar programs is
> costly, and the OO people are right to point to that cost.   But we don't
need
> to get the first customer to sign off on maintenance done to the second
> customer's program.  If a business rule changes for one customer, we don't
have
> to figure out a generic way to make it different for that customer - we
simply
> change that customer's program.

I won't act like I haven't done this, but a history of this type of code
creep leads
to such a mass of source code, that even simple changes become difficult
when
multiplied by 100.

> As I keep saying - each choice we make has some costs and some benefits.
The
> choice we make for one environment isn't always the same choice we would
make in
> a different environment.  And as costs change, environments change and we
should
> re-evaluate our decisions.

I agree whole-heartedly with this.  I suspect, however, that you're
over-rating the
cost of OO development.
```

at once.  While this means there is more coding (bad),  and more chances for
error (bad) - each error will effect fewer users (good), and each implementation
is more closely owned by the appropriate user (good).  In my environment, user
acceptance testing is much harder than coding or analysis.  Methodologies that
make user acceptance testing handleable are preferred over methodologies that
make programming easier.

> when multiplied by 100.

and utilities in these languages, as VB is much faster to develop interfaces
with.

VB falls short of OO in one critical feature--inheritance.  Polymorphism,
name spaces, encapsulation, data-hiding, messages are all mechanisms
familiar to VB.  VB also takes a hit as a real OO environment in that so
many of the internals are hidden from the developer.  Although that's a
valid criticism of VB, it's not technically a limit to it's OO
implementation.

Falling down is an overstatement--VB certainly seems to have legs.  It's
the nature of component development that the details of low-level
implementation
is hidden from the high-level developer.  This is also a strength of Cobol's
design.

Lotus Notes is a great example of the cost of having a fully-stocked
development environment--it comes with a horrible cost when attempting
to do something against the grain.  You can send a meeting invitation
and process the acceptances/declines with a few lines of code, but you'll
bleed from your ears trying to implement a custom print dialog.

It's true, though, that I prefer VC++, and especially Delphi because of the
availability of the infrastructure's code.

could
really use it, though, is in RDBMS access.  The resolution of a performance
bottleneck always seems to come in optimizing data access.

Grinder,

You are doing an excellent job with this debate...<G>

It is really refreshing to hear someone else presenting reasoned and
reasonable arguments in support of OO. Some of the people here are really
into OO COBOL, but are consistently met with walls of prejudice and
resistance to change when posting in this NG. (So much so that a private NG
on OO COBOL was started about a year ago... I'm sure that after your posts
here you will have been invited to join it.)

That is right and proper of course, because we all have our various
viewpoints and this is a public forum, but it can become tiresome explaining
something at length as part of a reasoned argument, then having to repeat
the whole process over again a few months later because some new arrivals
didn't see the last debate...

Similarly, OO COBOL has been an "emerging technology" for some time now and
the practitioners of it need to discourse and communicate about it without
having to worry about off topic wanderings and arguing about whether it is a
"Good Thing" or not. (So, that rules out this NG...<G>)

The bottom line is: ""OO will figure in the future of Programming whether
you like it or not."

It MUST, because it is just too sensible to be ignored.

The argument that it hasn't been picked up by Commercial Enterprises, sounds
somewhat hollow when  we see that these same Enterprises are switching to
Java and VB (which are both Object Oriented (although I accept the
reservations you pointed out for VB in this regard)) at the rate of knots...

But the main reason why I believe OO has a secure place in the future is
that it opens the box on components (Heavenly harps in
background...fluttering of white doves...<G>), and components are the Keys
to the Kingdom....

I have one (fairly long, but it is the only one I intend to make...<G>)
comment to your post, see below...

Pete.

> bottleneck always seems to come in optimizing data access.
>
I have spent the last 15 months building COM components with OO COBOL which
are primarily designed for fast access to RDBs. Obviously, much of this has
been leveraged from my own structured modular COBOL and applies to my own
Applications. Nevertheless, I am now starting to reap the benefits of this
effort.

I can now develop new systems which have "plug-in" sub-systems like Client
processing for instance. (Reuse courtesy of components). The same CLIENT
component uses other components to access the Client database. All the
standard functions (ADD, CHANGE, DELETE, VIEW, and SEARCH (on Soundex,
Generic Name substring, or Client number), and on-line and batch fuzzy logic
duplicate detection) are provided by PowerCOBOL OO code packaged as COM
components.
(The "Client database" mentioned above is just a series of RDB tables which
can integrate with any other Application RDB, and has "hooks" to provide
access using keys which may already exist in the new application. (Account
numbers, for instance))

A similar set of components provide the GUI user interface. (All Windows
based screens with full standard Windows functionality). Low level
components are activated for data entry of Dates and Numbers on GUI screens
(these can be attached to the "gained focus" event, so for instance, when
the User tabs to a date field on a data entry screen, a component causes an
instant pop-up calendar to appear and the date can be "point and clicked"
...if you don't like mice you can toggle to a keyboard entry pop-up (using a
Function key) which requires 4 key strokes (plus Enter) to input a date.
Whatever the User enters, whether it is by mouse or by keyboard, is
automatically passed to some internal Methods which leave a number of Public
Properties available to the Application... These properties provide the date
(validated and Millennium compliant for the next 25000 years) in any of 6
different formats, including the 64 bit floating point format used by VB or
ACCESS "DATE" type fields.

The main point is that the screen can show "20-Jan-2005" while the database
actually gets the same date as 20050120...(or the VB format if the field is
defined as type "Date" on the RDB...). The day of the Week is also provided
and you can actually pull down menus from the data entry pop-ups which will
allow input of "Next Thursday", "This Wednesday", "Today", "Tomorrow" etc.
All-in-all, comprehensive DATE input and processing by means of code that
never has to be debugged or thought about, and can be included in any number
of fields or screens by the click of a mouse in the IDE, but is run from a
single shared .DLL

The CLIENT Component operates at a much higher level.

To set up a new system which has "Customers", "Clients", "Patients",
"Members" "Employees" or any other kind of "Human revenue generating Object"
(the revenue generation is an optional component so "Employees" fits in
fine...<G>) I simply provide an ACCESS database (easily exportable to ORACLE
or DB2 or UDB)  with a series of .DLLs... The whole lot is accessed through
ODBC so it is open, portable, scalable, and is tuned to perform well. At the
moment, the User Interface is Windows based (the components described above)
but I am toying with using an independent Screen driver like SP2 at some
stage in the future. It is currently feasible to use the RDB components to
access data on a Mainframe. With the advent of .NET and Component support
for the mainframe, it should be possible to run these Components ON the
mainframe, and simply write a new User Interface in CICS or IMS/DC, if
required.

There is no comparison between designing systems around components, based on
WHAT they do, and the traditional method of designing systems around
sequential code based on WHEN is the best time to execute a given function.

Because the systems are Component based, there is a standard look and feel
to them. Numeric fields always get validated in the same way, Dates are
always entered with the same options and the displays always show a
consistent date format (I use the International "DD-Mmm-YYYY").

The logical extension of all this is Component based systems which can be
put together with a "smart" Visual IDE. (using Drag 'n' Drop to place
components and subsystem components onto a background "system development"
board and then have the development environment define the interfaces
between components, generate test data and validate the design. The User
will interact with it to check the output and provide goals that the system
will seek to attain.)

While commercial systems like this are not exactly just around the corner,
some are already available for Web Development, and enable people like
Graphic Designers (who may have no knowledge of computer programming or even
basic HTML) to build killer Web Sites based on their Artistic capability,
rather than their computer technical capability. It is only a matter of time
before pressure from the Market Place forces the same thing to happen in
Enterprises and we will see people with Business knowledge (rather than
Programming knowledge) selecting components to support the key systems of
their business.

Using Components, the design of a new system becomes a series of
interactions, brainstorms, exercises, paper models, and walkthroughs to
decide what Classes, Objects, Methods, Properties, and Events are important,
and then a quick look through the Component Library to decide what exists
and what needs to be developed or bought in.

All of it would be impossible without Object Oriented technology.

Get used to it. If you don't know anything about it, learn it. I recommend
learning Java for primarily this reason: It introduces OO concepts as a
Natural progression and an innate part of the Language.

You've invested years in COBOL; can it hurt to spend a few weeks learning
Java? (See the books I recommended elsewhere in this thread...you CAN learn
it in a few weeks...honest!)

Pete.

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

> The argument that it hasn't been picked up by Commercial Enterprises, sounds
> somewhat hollow when  we see that these same Enterprises are switching to
> Java and VB (which are both Object Oriented (although I accept the
> reservations you pointed out for VB in this regard)) at the rate of knots...

Are commercial enterprises switching their business applications to Java and
VB?   Or are they adding new applications for which they feel those tools are a
better fit?

This is a serious question.

Follow up questions could include:

Why don't they use CoBOL instead of VB for their GUI front ends?   Is it that
people who understand GUIs are VB programmers?  Or is that tool more appropriate
under its own strengths?

Why don't they use OO CoBOL for their business applications?   Is it that the
people who understand the business are not skilled in OO?   Is it that OO is
hard to implement in a mature environment which already contains thousands of
non OO programs?   Or is it that this tool is not quite the best?

What do new shops use to start up a business environment?  My guess is they
start off with a package and then add on to it.   If that package is OO CoBOL,
they will continue with it, and a couple of generations from now, when they are
huge, they will have huge OO CoBOL enterprise shops.  But what are these
packages written in?

We can come up with reasons that one methodology or another should be preferred
- but until we analyze why one is used or not used in enterprise environments,
our understanding is lacking.

I think I can offer some ideas on your questions:

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:3B2F6A37.14BD4946@brazee.net...
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:
>
> > The argument that it hasn't been picked up by Commercial Enterprises,
sounds
> > somewhat hollow when  we see that these same Enterprises are switching
to
> > Java and VB (which are both Object Oriented (although I accept the
> > reservations you pointed out for VB in this regard)) at the rate of
knots...
>
> Are commercial enterprises switching their business applications to Java
and
> VB?   Or are they adding new applications for which they feel those tools
are a
> better fit?

> This is a serious question.

I think both approaches are being used.  GUI front-ends are a natural target
for newer development methodologies because of their high visibility and the
suitability of OOP for GUI development.

OOP is still very valuable for non-GUI development, but existing systems
that
are mature and stable would have a slimmer margin of cost reduction--if
maintenance is low, cutting it half won't look that attractive when compared
to the cost of re-factoring it.  New development, on the other hand, gets
the
kick right from the start.

> Follow up questions could include:
>
> Why don't they use CoBOL instead of VB for their GUI front ends?   Is it
that
> people who understand GUIs are VB programmers?  Or is that tool more
appropriate
> under its own strengths?

There are a lot of factors in this.  I prefer the debate of OOP vs.
procedural,
rather than Cobol vs. VB/VC++, but these are the reasons why these tools
are so often the choice over OO Cobol.

(1)  Momentum - Rightly or wrongly, there is a huge community of support
      for VB/VC++ development.  OO Cobol, on the other hand is not nearly
      as pervasive.

(2)  Maturity - OO Cobol is well-reasoned, but because of it's relatively
low
      penetration, it's not had the shake-out and refinement that other OOP
      tools have experience.  (1) depends upon (2), and (2) upon (1)--so
this
      presents a sticking point.

(3)  Event-driven programming is not synonymous with OOP, but the models
      are more compatible.  Using an environment aimed at event-driven
      models is more natural that using one that has added it in.  There's
no
      reason why an OO/event Cobol can't be as effective as any other tool,
      it just has farther to go.

> Why don't they use OO CoBOL for their business applications?   Is it that
the
> people who understand the business are not skilled in OO?

Admittedly, this is a mystery to me.  There is a steeper learning curve for
OO, but it's compatible with more traditional programming.  Some businesses
are moving forward with OOP, but it doesn't seem to be with Cobol.  Other
than the aforementioned reasons, I don't see why this would be so.

>
Is it that OO is
> hard to implement in a mature environment which already contains thousands
of
> non OO programs?

There is definitely truth in this statement.  It just doesn't apply to OO,
though, I
think there's a comfort level in not touching a working system.  A
persuasive
argument must be made to project the long-term benefit of such an approach.

> Or is it that this tool is not quite the best?

I don't think there's anything wrong with any of these tools--in a vacuum.
In
the case of new development, though, the benefits of OOP are significant.

> What do new shops use to start up a business environment?  My guess is
they
> start off with a package and then add on to it.   If that package is OO
CoBOL,
> they will continue with it, and a couple of generations from now, when
they are
> huge, they will have huge OO CoBOL enterprise shops.  But what are these
> packages written in?

> We can come up with reasons that one methodology or another should be
preferred
> - but until we analyze why one is used or not used in enterprise
environments,
> our understanding is lacking.

Whenever I make a technology selection, I decompose it into a number of
factors.
On factor is straight technical merit--independent of the context.  I can't
deny that
context is often a siginficant consideration.  This includes existing code
base, availbility
of resources skilled in the technology, and a straight cost analysis of the
products
themselves.

I believe that the mixture of these considerations are hard to distill down
to a
reason that one tool is used over another.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-06-21T01:40:42+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3b30a87d_6@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <kBBW6.12199$I6.259641@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B2B5D24.D8F90EA9@brazee.net> <C5TW6.51820$r4.456780@e420r-atl3.usenetserver.com> <3B2E0108.C02C3CB3@brazee.net> <3OxX6.48741$u34.1913672@e420r-sjo2.usenetserver.com> <3B2EBAB2.9A82426A@home.com> <9MzX6.49083$u34.1981483@e420r-sjo2.usenetserver.com> <3b2f5533_1@Usenet.com> <3B2F6A37.14BD4946@brazee.net>`

```
Howard,

You have long been one of the "Voices of Reason" in this Newsgroup and I
know that your enquiry is genuine. I also understand that you have many
reservations about the use of OO. Given that it is you who is asking, I have
been intending to respond to your points for some time, not in an
adversarial way, but simply examining the implications of the points you
raised.

Grinder is also genuine in his approach and has posted valid responses  so I
don't want to re-invent his wheel...

I have left the day-to-day nuts and bolts arguments regarding OO from a
programming perspective in his very capable hands, and will confine myself
to the more "Management Oriented" implications arising from your questions.

My response below therefore should be read in conjunction with his
response...

I stress that everything I have written below is simply my own opinion about
it, but it is based on actual experience implementing VB and Java solutions
(as well as  non-OO COBOL) in corporations.

Pete.
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:3B2F6A37.14BD4946@brazee.net...
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:
>
> > The argument that it hasn't been picked up by Commercial Enterprises,
sounds
> > somewhat hollow when  we see that these same Enterprises are switching
to
> > Java and VB (which are both Object Oriented (although I accept the
> > reservations you pointed out for VB in this regard)) at the rate of
knots...
>>>
> Are commercial enterprises switching their business applications to Java
and
> VB?   Or are they adding new applications for which they feel those tools
are a
> better fit?
>

It is a very good question. I don't believe that commercial concerns tailor
their applications to the tools available, but I agree it is arguable. (I
would support the viewpoint that applications need to be implemented, and we
should use the best tools available to do it; the tools are the "tail" and
the tail does not wag the dog...)

My own experience has been in using VB front ends as a "quick build" GUI
interface to a COBOL driven mainframe back end (UDB and DB2). Tools such as
Rumba have also been used to carry out the interfacing. I am firmly
persuaded as to the efficacy of the multi tier approach, and VB is a good
tool for the Presentation layer. By the same token, Mainframe COBOL is an
effective tool for the bottom layer Data Access.

This then raises the question of where OO fits in. I have not personally
implemented OO solutions in any Corporate environment, but it is around 2
and a half years since I last worked in such an environment. The last time I
managed a project where we used VB as a front end was in 1998.  In the
meantime, I have been exploring OO, learning OO COBOL and Java, and
developing Components and PC based Windows software. This software has been
developed primarily for my own amusement (and use, where tools are
concerned) and has (almost by accident) turned out to be marketable. I am
now persuaded that OO is a very viable approach (components are even better)
and I intend to use it in the next projects I manage at a Corporate level.
(I am going back to "work" in October and I'll let you know how we get
on...<G>).

Java was originally used for Web Enablement, but as the skill sets and
understanding of OO concepts gelled, it has been moved out into standard
application development in many Corporations. I think you will find that the
places where Java is being used for application development are, in the
main, places which started using it for their Intranet and Web development,
then realised that Java Classes can also be used for application
development. This is like extending the "applet mentality" to Standard
Classes.

My own take on this is that Java is not necessarily the BEST tool to do this
with, but people are finding that the "Class/Method/ Property/Event"
paradigm (OO, if you like...) is very workable. (The people finding this
are, for the most part, NOT COBOL programmers (who find most OO a mystery)
but the success of Java in certain areas encourages Management to use it in
other areas (which may not be the best places to use it...)

Bear in mind that, due to the non existence of a viable standard for OO
COBOL in the Market Place, they had NO option OTHER than to use Java (or
C++, but that has proven to be virtually non-viable for serious COMMERCIAL
development due to the difficulty of maintaining it) if they wanted to
implement OO Applications.

This is changing. (No, I promised myself not to get started on the bloody
standards (or lack of them) again...<G>). There WILL be a viable standard in
the comparatively near future. Even if there isn't, or if we don't care
about it, many Vendors are already implementing OO COBOL so there IS an
alternative to using Java for commercial OO application development.

(Please don't go racing off and say: "Great! I'll use COBOL for my Web
Development...I can write CGI and ISAPI gateway code in COBOL with a
multi-threaded Run time and it will kill Java...!!! Nope. You CAN do it. But
it does not have the flexibility or scalability of Java Servlets in this
environment. It's a bit like deciding to write the Sales Analysis reports in
C++... you COULD do it, but WOULD you?) "BEST tools for the job"...

The alternative to using Java for commercial OO application development (OO
COBOL) just isn't credible....yet. (This is not to undermine or denigrate
Java; it is STILL the best tool for Web based development (JavaScript is
useful, but Java Applets are still better, in my opinion). That's why I have
consistently advocated the use of BOTH COBOL AND Java as complementary to
each other.

Summarised answer to your question:

Corporations are switching to Java and VB (in the cases where they are)
because they have had success in using these tools for quick build GUI front
ends and for Web Enablement. Corporations doing this are already "sold" on
the use of OO, but they have had no viable alternative with which to
implement it for application development.

I believe this will change, and we will see a gradual take up of OO COBOL,
not to REPLACE Java, but to COMPLEMENT it...<G>. I further believe that
Corporations not yet "sold" on OO will gradually be persuaded. The use of
"off the shelf" components, the advent of a common runtime, and the savings
which can be made with the OO paradigm, will accelerate this process.

(I agree that there have been "bad" implementations of OO (and component
technology) but these are the "teething troubles" you can expect from any
new technology when it is implemented under pressure with incomplete
understanding and little experience. As the learning curve steepens, systems
get better.

Experience is the best teacher (but her fees are very high...<G>))

>>>>
> Why don't they use CoBOL instead of VB for their GUI front ends?   Is it
that
> people who understand GUIs are VB programmers?  Or is that tool more
appropriate
> under its own strengths?
>
<<<
See discussion above. I use and LOVE GUI COBOL, but I wouldn't recommend it
into a Corporate environment at the moment. I don't want to go into my
reasons for this here and now, but I hope the situation will change and I
can alter my position on this. I need to explore some other GUI options
first.

VB is an excellent tool for GUI front ends and has proven itself to be both
simple and stable.

>>>
> Why don't they use OO CoBOL for their business applications?   Is it that
the
> people who understand the business are not skilled in OO?   Is it that OO
is
> hard to implement in a mature environment which already contains thousands
of
> non OO programs?   Or is it that this tool is not quite the best?
<<<
The people who understand OO are not COBOL people. The COBOL people see no
reason to change from what they consider to be a successful Methodology.
(Many shops are still using the Waterfall, and STILL believe it is the BEST
approach. I'll be covering this at CW2001 (hope it does some good...<G>)).

The people who understand the Business are not skilled in OO or any other
kind of COBOL. The use of Objects DOES allow the programmers and Business
people to communicate more effectively, but, as noted elsewhere, the COBOL
people are not currently into the Object Oriented approach...

It will all take time and the Market waits for no man...hence the fact that
COBOL could well miss the boat...

As for "a mature environment which already contains thousands of non OO
programs", even as you read this, some VERY clever tools are being developed
and refined for factoring the business rules out of Legacy code. I believe
this code will eventually be refactored into components which will be
reused.

I understand your scepticism; I too have seen some of the horrific crap that
resides in Corporate Source Libraries...<G> but, as the necessity level
rises and the only other option is to redevelop, the cost of salvaging this
stuff and making it reusable becomes more attractive. Couple that with new
techniques for analysing text (program source, especially) and processing
power that would've been unthinkable even a decade ago, and what looked
"impossible" becomes viable. Time will tell, but I would ask you to reserve
judgement on this for a little while...

 >>>>
> What do new shops use to start up a business environment?  My guess is
they
> start off with a package and then add on to it.
<<<
Your intuition and observation are right on the button here, Howard.

More and more "new" businesses, called to set up an IT "shop" will opt for
packages (witness the rise of SAP and  Siebel, to name just 2...Personally,
I am not happy with either of  these "solutions" (although SAP has improved
out of sight since I first reviewed it in Germany in the late seventies or
early eighties, can't remember exactly...), however, whatever my personal
opinion may be, they represent  a minimum  financial risk strategy that many
Senior Management find attractive.

Some will assemble their own "packages" using outsourced components, fewer
and fewer will go for building and maintaining their own applications from
the ground up.

>  If that package is OO CoBOL,
> they will continue with it, and a couple of generations from now, when
they are
> huge, they will have huge OO CoBOL enterprise shops.  But what are these
> packages written in?
>
Well, it's hardly fair to call OO COBOL a "package", but, accepting it for
the sake of this discussion, then a couple of generations from now they will
have an OO COBOL shop more or less the same size as they started with.

How will they manage this?

By buying components and using their own people purely for the task of
"gluing" them together. This will be done in close conjunction with the
Business (forget the Waterfall, it doesn't work now, never has, and it never
will...besides, there are better ways to manage development) and specialist
"Agents" seconded from pertinent areas of the Business, temporarily, for the
duration of specific application development.

As the component library expands, the re-use of the code obviates the need
for expanding the IT department (at least on the scale we have seen
historically for sequential code development). It is a DIFFERENT ball game
to the COBOL we know and love now, and it is made possible by the use of the
OO paradigm.

(think about it...any given Business can be resolved into a finite number of
Objects communicating with each other. It may be a large number, but it IS
finite... and they crop up again and again in different "flavours" in
different industries...this is exactly the stuff that OO dreams are made
of...)

Eventually, "smart" software will enable the Business to "glue" their own
applications together, and then the IT department will become even smaller
and consist mainly of a few Network specialists, a general purpose
professional programmer or two (proficient in several languages and TOTALLY
fluent in OO and component technology) a few DBAs, some Business Liaison
Analysts, and a Help Desk....

As software gets smarter and smarter, the need for technical expertise gets
lesser and lesser, hence natural shrinkage in the IT department. I know it
isn't very apparent TODAY, when everybody is screaming for IT skill, but I'm
not talking about TODAY.

History has shown that when a demand is created, supply rises to meet it,
driven by the resulting Market Place. I can't see 400,000,000 computer users
all becoming programmers, yet they will demand responsive systems and the
ability to "tailor" their systems by interacting with them (A loose
definition of "programming"...?). Just as an indicator, MicroSoft are
reputedly budgeting several BILLION dollars for the Operating systems of the
future in order to make them more "smart" and less technical...

>>>
> We can come up with reasons that one methodology or another should be
preferred
> - but until we analyze why one is used or not used in enterprise
environments,
> our understanding is lacking.
>
<<<
Yes, I agree. I hope that the above thoughts will stimulate further
discussion on this. The Market is a dynamic place and things change very
quickly. Here are my predictions:

1. OO COBOL will achieve growth within the next five years OR COBOL will be
extinct within seven.
(in 12 years COBOL will be as relevant as Latin or Swahili. Don't
worry...some people will continue writing it just for fun...I intend to be
one of them...<G>)

2. The emphasis in system development will move from SOURCE code to OBJECT
code (components).
(These will have known, encapsulated, functionality, with standard
interfaces. They will do what they are supposed to do, and will not be
"maintained" in the sense that we currently maintain source code.)

3. The mainframe environment will support component development and running,
within 4 years. This, in turn, will stimulate interest in OO techniques
amongst mainframers. (Client/server and PC people are already into it...)

4. The current boundaries between mainframe and Client/server, Windows and
Unix, and phones, TV and the Internet will become so blurred as to be
indistinguishable. A major contributor to this will be the acceptance of a
common runtime and the universal development of components which use this
runtime. New Operating Systems will be "smarter" and harder to distinguish
from one another...

5. Existing Legacy code WILL be leveraged, factored, and re-invented, using
the component based design model. Tools to do this will become available and
even in cases where you think it can't be done, it will be (only exception
would be where existing components duplicate it already).

OK, that's it for now. I have taken some time to address the points you
raised, Howard, because they are good points and I know your interest is
genuine. I hope this post will stimulate further thought and discussion. (I
have been deliberately provocative in some areas, but never to the point of
saying something I don't believe or cannot support.)

OO is here to stay. "With" or "without" COBOL, depends on factors which we
have limited control over (attitudes of programmers, availability of a
Standard, Vendor implementations, etc.)  I hope it is "with"...

Pete.

```

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com> wrote in message
news:3B2F81BF.24B3CCC6@home.com...
>
> I buy all that you are saying - but the following :-
>
> >
> > Because the systems are Component based, there is a standard look and
feel
> > to them. Numeric fields always get validated in the same way, Dates are
> > always entered with the same options and the displays always show a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> I understood the ISO format to be YYYYMMDD = YYYY Mmm DD ? (I need hardly
add,
> here in Canada we are totally 'confused' - no doubt because of US or Brit
> influence, dates pop out at you as Mmm dd YYYY,  dd Mmm YYYY, and
government
> wise, (provincially and federal),  they often go for YYYYmmdd).
>
> Jimmy

Here's a statement of how I see this problem:

"Human Beings need to have a Date format which tells them IMMEDIATELY,
without any requirement for calculation or excessive thought, what day it is
(in the context of the current year and century). Such a format is NOT
useful to a computer and requires conversion before it can be used in
calculations."

Over the years, working with various Humans and computers, I have found that
even the mainframe date format (YY/MM/DD or DD/MM/YY, depending on where you
live on the planet, and modified since Y2K in many places to now be
YYYY/MM/DD or DD/MM/YYYY) is NOT immediately intuitive to many Humans.

For example, less than 50% of people can INSTANTLY say what the 8th month
is, with total certainty. (The best I ever saw was a person who counted with
her tongue on her teeth and came up with "September"...)

So, when it came to developing PC applications over which, as the developer,
I have full control, I decided very early on that ALL displays and reports
(anything designed to be viewed by a Human) would be in the "Human friendly"
format: DD-Mmm-YYYY.
(There is a small spin-off from this in that the month can be provided in
the Natural Language of the country where the application is being
implemented, without any real effort on my part.
20-Mar-2001 works nicely whether you are English, French, or German,
20-Mai-2001 works fine if you are French, but requires 20-May-2001 for the
English and German speakers. Using OO it is no problem to provide both, and
that is why, after working on the whole DATE interaction for a number of
years, I am now satisfied that this problem is completely solved and can be
put to bed, the acquired wisdom and experience being encapsulated as a
Component.)

Feedback from users (many of whom had only ever had DD/MM/YY) was positive,
so I continued this practice.

However, when it comes to calculation, DD-Mmm-YYYY is not a very amenable
format. And what about feeding other systems which require a different
format? I soon realised that a "Universal Converter" was required so I wrote
one. You give it a date in ANY of 6 formats. All of the formats you mention
above are easily presentable to it, plus some other, more computer oriented
ones, like the 64 bit VB format, the days since 1900, the YYYMMDD format,
and so on... It returns ALL the formats (fills in the blanks).

(The latest version will accept ANYTHING that purports to be a date and
doesn't care about the format Give it a string with hyphens, slashes,
spaces, text, whatever...this is part of my ongoing experimentation to
develop "smart" components... I'm still working on it and it keeps me off
the streets...<G>)

The component which pops up a calendar or a keyboard entry box when you TAB
to a date input field incorporates all of the above. It gets the User input,
validates it, notifies the User if it is invalid and tries again, then makes
the entered date available to your application in the 6 formats mentioned,
some of which are "Human oriented", some of which are "computer oriented",
and all of which can be used to easily derive any other date format which
may be required. My applications simply pick up the DD-Mmm-YYYY format and
place it in the field which triggered the process, then move the cursor on
to the next field.

It is User friendly and my customers love it.

I use the other formats internally to provide Dates for interfacing to other
external systems and databases.

Finally, a brief comment on your point regarding ISO standards for dates,
Jimmy.

"If a Standards authority takes forever to come up with something useful,
and in the meantime, as a developer, I need to provide a service to my
customers, forgive me if I don't wait until the Standard is available.
Instead, I'll talk to my customers and find out what is needed and wanted.
Having done that, I'll provide it. This is a Marketing strategy which works
for me...

By agonizing too long, a standard can become out of touch with the Market it
is intended to standardize, and developers will simply ignore it and rely on
the feedback from their customer base.

It is hardly surprising if the "Standard" gets "sidelined"..."

If I was to suggest to my user base that all dates were going to be changed
to comply with an ISO standard, do you think it would be a good career
move...?

Pete.

Well, this time you raise a very valid point, Jimmy.

The Standard was in existence at the time I approached this problem and I
just failed to check it out.

As a result, all my software violates this Standard.

Gee, I guess no-one's going to buy it now...

So the component that does this is obviously useless. (Well, I guess you
COULD take the DD-Mmm-YYYY  or possibly the YYYYMMDD format, and reformat it
to comply with the Standard before displaying it... but the component will
need to have the ISO Standard date format added to its repertoire.)

Wow, I'm shattered by this, after the all the hours I put into it...

Tell you what, I'll GIVE it away free to anyone who wants it...

If you have a Fujitsu RTS (V4 or above) on your system, are experimenting
with OO COBOL and would like to try using a component to handle all your
date processing, mail me and I'll send it to you. Be aware it doesn't comply
with the ISO date standard....awww, shucks, I'll fix it so it does before I
send it to you...there y' go..<G>

Despite the irony above, if I  (or, much more importantly,  my users) really
thought the Standard was a more attractive format, I would issue an upgrade
in a heartbeat. (all I have to do is replace one component .DLL).

The fact is that (to my eyes at least) it is uglier than what I am currently
using. But even worse, it doesn't meet the criterion defined in my previous
post, that says a "Human-friendly" date format must be IMMEDIATELY
discernible, without calculation or extra mental effort. "yyyy-mm-dd" STILL
requires the month ordinal position to be translated mentally to the actual
month. (Remember the girl who counted with her tongue on her teeth...?)

So I have to take a decision: Do I comply with the Standard because it is a
standard? Or, do I let it ride because everyone's happy with the status quo
and it will cost (albeit, minimally) to change it?

Votes via snail mail on the back of a $10 bill, please... (yeah, right...!)

Pete.

> Actually, I am more concerned with scalability than being pure OO.

You're interested in how OOP scales compared to procedural
code, or your just more interested in approaches to manage
a scaling application?

> I suspect we have different definitions of "large application".

The metric is hard to pick--lines of code doesn't seem to be
the best.  Function points are good, but variously applied. I'll
take a shot though.  The large VB application I referred to
has:

* 150 forms, ranging from about 20 to 250 controls per form.
* 100 or so business objects, and about 50 other utility-type
   objects.
* 120 tables, ranging from about 6 to 60 fields per table.
* 200 stored procedures

The cobol application I would rate at medium:

* 15 EXEs and about 120 DLLs
* about 70 panels, but this number will increase as
   we deepen the GUI implementation
```

Before someone jumps on this and says "then why have few gone OO on
the mainframe....

The IBM OO implementation is a bare minimum.  It doesn't support
"properties" or the "factory" and a host of other things I find
necessary to make this even close to useable.  So while the mainframe
(IBM) has OO extensions I would not call the offering an OO compiler.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Scalability (was: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-19T17:39:34-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gokad$p7n$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <kBBW6.12199$I6.259641@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B2B5D24.D8F90EA9@brazee.net> <C5TW6.51820$r4.456780@e420r-atl3.usenetserver.com> <3B2E0108.C02C3CB3@brazee.net> <3OxX6.48741$u34.1913672@e420r-sjo2.usenetserver.com> <3B2F4E18.3D47CA12@brazee.net> <FbNX6.1$Nb.132@e420r-atl2.usenetserver.com>`

```
To me "scalability" of applications depends much more on the number/size of
"transactions" to be processed than it does the size/complexity of its
"components".

I don't (personally) care if an application consists of 1 monolithic
program, 150 sub-programs, or all sorts of "objects/methods" - when I am
talking about scalability.

What I "care about is"

How many "users" must have concurrent access?

How many "records" (tables, etc) and of what size need to be processed in
what time frame
  (sub-second response time?  nightly batch run of multiple steps, whatever)

What are the "network" requirements (coordination, "mirroring of
information", reliability/backups, audit-trails, etc).

   ***

Most of this reflects my IBM mainframe background, but is how *I* think
about scalability.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

```

>subset of OO, so OO would be OO-ish and more.

to distinguish it as a not fully object-oriented language.

R. Miller

- **Date:** 2001-06-18T17:32:04+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B2E3AC9.4B3D37A3@home.com>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <kBBW6.12199$I6.259641@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B2B5D24.D8F90EA9@brazee.net>`

```


Howard Brazee wrote:

> > Indeed this comparison is imbalanced, as it takes significantly less OO code
> > than its inlined counterpart.
>
> I have to ask people who use OO CoBOL - how true is this statement?
>

Howard,

I see that Grinder gave the following response to the above question :-

>Third party experience is of interest to me, but I've seen this work
>already.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>of the code.

I confess I was quizzical about his statement. I don't think there is a major
difference between OO and non-OO in this respect. A business problem is the same
regardless of methodology, and you still have to check using  conditionals  IF...
and EVALUATE ...... Within OO you can 'chop up' the problem by making it into
separate methods - some need only be one line of code. But you can do exactly the
same in non-OO, introducing more perform PARAGRAPHS and even one line PARAGRAPHS.

One advantage OO does give you is being able to use a common method-name, e.g.,
"get-firstname" and throw it at different classes/objects and get the same result.
This of course allows you to 'carbon copy' your checks. (But not with multiple
inheritance - unless Thane gives me the solution <G>).

One other issue - which I think Ross mentioned - the 'haystack approach'. Ignoring
the GUI classes in Net Express, (which are not part of the proposed standard), as
yet in designing, I haven't gone beyond three deep in an hierarchy. I think the same
applies with non-OO; I wouldn't have thought most would get beyond a third level of
calling sub-programs.

In theory you can do it in both, but it results in Ross'es 'haystack'. More
importantly, while a 'clever' programmer may achieve it - us lesser mortals really
can't absorb more than three levels. Your advocacy of maintainability (OO and
non-OO), gets really screwed up if you can't easily comprehend what structure the
whiz kid designer introduced.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

- **From:** "Grinder" <no.spam@no.spam.spam.spam.net>
