```
 > > Indeed this comparison is imbalanced, as it takes significantly less OO
code
> > > than its inlined counterpart.
> >
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> >To be fair, the original code had some dead wood in it.  Some related
units
> >(yet untouched) will benefit, though, from the re-factored code.  I
suspect
> >that a large scale attack on the applications would yield a significant
> >concentration
> >of the code.
>
> I confess I was quizzical about his statement. I don't think there is a
major
> difference between OO and non-OO in this respect. A business problem is
the same
> regardless of methodology, and you still have to check using  conditionals
IF...
> and EVALUATE ...... Within OO you can 'chop up' the problem by making it
into
> separate methods - some need only be one line of code. But you can do
exactly the
> same in non-OO, introducing more perform PARAGRAPHS and even one line
PARAGRAPHS.

I think the distinction between a re-usable component and a general-use
paragraph is scope.  You can easily use the same code to perform for
different
suscribers without putting those suscribers in the same scope.

Our procedural program is comprised of many small programs.  If we want
the same message box in a number of those apps, we end up plugging that
code into each program, instead of instancing a component.

Even if you put the message box into a subprogram, morphing that code will
have to be done by duplicating the subprogram, rather than deriving a new
one.
It's the derivations that are so code efficient, because you're only adding
the
code to manage the difference--not the whole behavior.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Separately compiled and loaded modules was Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca>
- **Date:** 2001-06-22T00:19:44+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net>`

```
The COBOL dynamic call on the mainframe has exactly the same drawbacks
of immediately changing for all invokers of a given called module when
that module changes.  The last two mainframe shops and the HP-UX shop I
am currently at are all using dynamic calls at least in batch.  The
major questions I have about OO are the questions I have about the use
of common subroutines.

1.  What is the overhead of having separately loaded modules and does it
matter for the target environment?  There is a measurable overhead but
it is trivial for the applications that I have seen.

2.  How do we know that we have the sub-routine (object, etc.) and how
do we know what it does?  This is more of a question of the development
discipline in the given shop and if a shop handles code re-use and
common sub-routines well, it probably will be able to handle objects
well.

3.  Because of percieved greater flexibility in some ways, will there be
more checking and conversion done at the inter-module boundary?

4.  From what I have seen, the general rule is to pass the elements
needed separately that are needed by a called module and even more so by
an invoked object.  I question the practicality of this for items which
require a large number of elements such as state tax calculations where
the number and wierdness varies by state (and other jurisdictions).  

Clark Morris, CFM Technical Programming Services, Inc.
     cfmtech@istar.ca or clark.morris@ns.sympatico.ca  

Howard Brazee wrote:
> 
> "Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> costs.  So the cost/benefit ratio of going OO are different again.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Separately compiled and loaded modules was Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-06-22T22:13:52+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3b331a06_4@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca>`

```
"Clark F. Morris, Jr." <cfmtech@istar.ca> wrote in message
news:3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca...
> The COBOL dynamic call on the mainframe has exactly the same drawbacks
> of immediately changing for all invokers of a given called module when
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Don't confuse dynamic mainframe calls with instantiating Objects via a
constructor method. There is no comparison. The OO Factory method is already
loaded in memory and churns out instances of data when it is requested to
(via "NEW"). When Object methods are invoked they are applied to this
instance data, via the Object Reference which was returned from the
instantiation. Logically, the process can be visualised as similar to
dynamically loading a module, but that is where the similarity ends.
Physically the processes are quite dissimilar.

>>>
> 1.  What is the overhead of having separately loaded modules and does it
> matter for the target environment?  There is a measurable overhead but
> it is trivial for the applications that I have seen.
>
<<<
That isn't how it works... see above.

>>>
> 2.  How do we know that we have the sub-routine (object, etc.) and how
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
<<<
When it is instantiated it returns an Object Reference to itself. If you
instantiate again you get another instance and another Object Reference. You
know "what it does" from its documented Methods and Properties. Hence you
access the Properties via the same Object Reference as you access the Object
Methods. It is like a Universal Pointer to ONE PARTICULAR instance of the
Object. There is nothing to stop you having an array of Object References
which all represent the same CLASS of Object. However, each one will
represent a different instance of it. Imagine a litho plate which prints a
picture; each print is numbered, but they are all identical. If you want to
mark one of the pictures you refer to it by its number. It is exactly the
same principle.

>>>
> 3.  Because of percieved greater flexibility in some ways, will there be
> more checking and conversion done at the inter-module boundary?
>
<<<
Yes. The new OO COBOL standard (grimace) calls for conformance checking at
compile time to ensure that the parameters passed to an Object's methods are
valid for it. (It is similar to the use of Header files in C++). OO COBOL
defines a Repository which enables it to do this checking, but (in my
opinion) a better way is to define Method Prototypes which can be used as
"stubs" while you are developing a system. (I don't know what the Standard
says about this, but it is common practice in other languages and Fujitsu OO
COBOL supports it, so it gets my vote...)

>>>
> 4.  From what I have seen, the general rule is to pass the elements
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> the number and wierdness varies by state (and other jurisdictions).
<<<
Well I can't comment on what you've seen, but I can assure you I have
objects which pass "structures" (Group Properties, if you like) when this is
desirable. If you wanted to make such Properties individually available as
Public Properties, you would need to define them as Public Properties and
set them within the Factory Method by moving elements of the passed
structure to the individual fields. (I don't do that, but it is quite
feasible and avoids the need to make every Property a separate parameter.)

You may have seen this process arise because of some misunderstandings
regarding the nature of COM objects. Many people believe that for a COM
object, every property must be defined and requires a GET or SET Method.
This rules out the passing of structures. However, this belief is fallacious
and I have COM components whch pass structures as COM type VT_STRING and
work fine.

So to answer your question, what you have seen is not "wrong" but it is
unnecessary. A "large number of elements such as state tax calculations
where the number and wierdness varies by state (and other jurisdictions)"
could be passed as an array of type "String".

From the theoretical point of view it would be better if the Object went and
fetched them off a database or from a Collection; or maybe a "collector"
object could be invoked, depending on the jurisdiction, and then pass what
it collected as an array to the actual calculation module. This has to do
with encapsulation and visibility of data to processes which don't require
it.

Hope this helps,

Pete.



Posted Via Usenet.com Premium Usenet Newsgroup Services
----------------------------------------------------------
    ** SPEED ** RETENTION ** COMPLETION ** ANONYMITY **
----------------------------------------------------------        
                http://www.usenet.com
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Learning OO COBOL

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-22T18:37:22+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B339005.721A88B7@home.com>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca>`

```

Clark wrote :

> The COBOL dynamic call on the mainframe has exactly the same drawbacks
> of immediately changing for all invokers of a given called module when
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> it is trivial for the applications that I have seen.

> 2.  How do we know that we have the sub-routine (object, etc.) and how
> do we know what it does?  This is more of a question of the development
> discipline in the given shop and if a shop handles code re-use and
> common sub-routines well, it probably will be able to handle objects
> well.

> 3.  Because of percieved greater flexibility in some ways, will there be
> more checking and conversion done at the inter-module boundary?
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Firstly I think Pete gave an enjoyably readable and informative answer to Howard's
original questions, plus he also briefly covered your 'genuine concerns'.
Independently, I started penning a reply to you - dammit - it got so long it almost
turned into a theses !

As Pete said, while we appreciate 'others interest' we really can't devote numerous
hours to putting the OO ideas across. As we have seen in the past there is very real
hostility to OO from many in the COBOL ranks - I'm afraid they must be classified as
Luddites. It's worth noting that those of us writing here about OO are several decades
beyond cutting our milk teeth ! Therefore we are hardly likely to be seduced by new
techniques just for the hell of it. (I'll qualify that - if her name is J Lo, Faith
Hill or Christine Aguila - then Miss Seduction will definitely get my attention - and
I'm not thinking about their singing abilities <G>).

So for those who genuinely are truly, truly interested in getting a handle on OO COBOL
- even if they never finish up using it in their working life, some suggested sources
:-

1. Go to the J4 site and download a copy of the Standard (CD 1.10). Go to Annex C
starting at para C.17 (Page 767) and it is a pretty decent explanation of OO and how
it is to be implemented in COBOL. It finishes up with some simple source code
illustrating the major principles. Obviously it can't totally cover all the 'ins' and
'outs' but it does a worthwhile job. My only regret is that the Standard document
hasn't segregated OO as a separate entity - further up in the document OO verbs/syntax
get mixed in alphabetically with non-OO - which might be OK for compiler vendors'
staff, but just doesn't make it easy for me to read as a reference document.

2. Books - I've said it before, but will mention them again. At this point in time
there are only two that are worthwhile - because having stated principles they then
follow through with coded examples which WORK !. Both use the Net Express version of
OO, which ignoring Repository syntax, (which Pete just referred to), doesn't make the
early Micro Focus implementation dramatically out of step with the proposed standard.
The two books :-

1. "Elements of OO COBOL" - Wilson (Will) Price - available from cobolreport.com. Both
Pete and Don Tees have given this one a Gold Star. Tutorial in style, starts with a
conventional non-OO program and leads you gently from there.

Will restarts each topic (chapter) picking up an additional aspect of OO - there's
even a brief coverage of DB and ESQL. He has done an excellent job. (It's not actually
in his book, but something he gave me separately as a white paper and diskette source
- the concept of COBOL file handling that I have turned into workable code).

I believe the old codger, (there's only a month or two difference in our ages), still
has it in him to write yet another book - before we both trot off into the sunset.
(Patience up THERE, I wasn't thinking of 'checking out' just yet).

2. "OO COBOL"  Edmund (Ed) C. Arranga and Frank P. Coyle. Available from SIGS Books &
Multimedia - SIGS - ISBN 1-884842-34-8 (pbk.) - http//www.sigs.com, or Prentice Hall -
ISBN 0-13-261140-6 . If you have trouble getting it - could always e-mail Ed at
cobolreport.com and see if he has a supply in his attic ! Note that Will and Ed are
partners in (objectz)cobolreport.com - hardly surprising - they are both on the same
wavelength.

The Arranga/Coyle book does an excellent job. It's the first one I picked up on and
gave me my personal 'Epiphany' - a term I purloined from Ed because he said exactly
the same thing when the penny dropped for him as regards OOP. Not tutorial like Will's
book, but definitely a MUST book they way it explains OO topics. If you like, it is an
excellent scenic backdrop to Will's  'family snapshots'  in his book. At the end of
the book is a working example of a Library application being run by a corporation for
their own staff. Doesn't get into file handling but uses the DB as tables in
working-storage.

Incidentally if you make it to CW2001 at Anaheim in October, all three authors, Ed,
Frank and Will, will be there.

I can tell you which books NOT to buy - but in deference to the authors' efforts - I
wont mention them. They just don't appear in my list above. There is one exception
"COBOL Unleashed" - has a very good chapter on OO - but you wouldn't buy the book just
for the chapter on OO anyway.

Simply put - OO syntax is not tremendously difficult to get a handle on. What is
EXTREMELY difficult is to comprehend how the concepts can literally be turned on their
heads, allowing you an amazing degree of flexibility in arriving at business
solutions, way beyond non-OO COBOL, although with some degree of effort you can
'parallel' some aspects with structured.

A closing thought -  if this retiree can get a handle on it and use it, sat here in
splendid isolation, then for those with a wealth of COBOL  experience or even the
bright eyed, bushy tailed youngsters coming along, then they should be able to do the
same.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

```
"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@home.com> wrote in message
news:3B339005.721A88B7@home.com...
>
> As Pete said, while we appreciate 'others interest' we really can't devote
numerous
> hours to putting the OO ideas across.

Personally, I'm quite happy to discuss OO (or anything else) publicly and
HAVE devoted numerous hours to doing so...<G>

I don't see it as my mission to evangelize OO (or anything else...you are
well aware of my personal beliefs, Jimmy)... as I said before "OO will take
over the World without any help from me...".

> As we have seen in the past there is very real
> hostility to OO from many in the COBOL ranks - I'm afraid they must be
classified as
> Luddites.

I don't see resistance to new ideas as being a Luddite (except in the case
of Foodman...<G>).

It is right and proper that people should investigate new stuff before
making up their minds. Sometimes the reaction is to feel threatened
(especially when the new idea is powerful and can be seen to be sweeping
through the environment.) This fear comes from not wanting to change what is
comfortable and successful, and, when you think it about it, is perfectly
understandable.

You have spent a lot of time learning something that is working quite nicely
for you and putting bread on your table, clothes on your back, and a roof
over your head, then some idiot comes along and suggests that what you have
is far from perfect and should be chucked out in favour of his new pet
theory...

Six months down the track, yet another Joshua comes along, blows his horn
and everybody follows HIM off into the Wilderness... It is small wonder that
there is suspicion and resistance in the ranks of COBOLlers...

All I will say is that OO represents a genuine threshold in the Evolution of
the way we program computers.

It has nothing to do with which Language is better or what the latest
fashion is, it is akin to the discovery of fire or the invention of the
wheel...Life will never be the same again.

(Even after fire was invented there were probably numerous cavepersons who
still preferred raw meat and kept warm by huddling together as they always
had, and I bet there were many who saw no use for the wheel as they didn't
want to go anywhere... And, of course, there would have been the Dilworths
of the cave who resisted all innovation as being downright unGodly and
against their Religion and putting the young on the Highway to Hell...)

OO has opened the door to components (sounds of "Allelujah Chorus" from
above...) and this is the most significant single event in the history of
computer programming in my lifetime.

And I've seen a few that were SUPPOSED to be...who could forget "Jackson
Structured Programming" - (well, after intensive therapy, I've managed to
forget most of it...<G>) or "The Last One" which was supposed to be the end
of computer programming... (followed some months later by "The Next One"
<G>), or PL/1 (originally, PL/I for "international" but when it came a
cropper, hastily changed to "1" as being the first incarnation of it. We're
still waiting for PL/2...<G>). Actually, it isn't really fair to include
PL/1 in the list because it is a very good and useful Language and I've
written stuff in it which would be difficult to do in COBOL...

Object Oriented Methodology is an altogether different proposition.

> It's worth noting that those of us writing here about OO are several
decades
> beyond cutting our milk teeth ! Therefore we are hardly likely to be
seduced by new
> techniques just for the hell of it. (I'll qualify that - if her name is J

> I'm not thinking about their singing abilities <G>).

suggested sources

>
> 1. Go to the J4 site and download a copy of the Standard (CD 1.10). Go to
Annex C
> starting at para C.17 (Page 767) and it is a pretty decent explanation of
OO and how
> it is to be implemented in COBOL. It finishes up with some simple source
code
> illustrating the major principles. Obviously it can't totally cover all
the 'ins' and
> 'outs' but it does a worthwhile job. My only regret is that the Standard
document
> hasn't segregated OO as a separate entity - further up in the document OO
verbs/syntax
> get mixed in alphabetically with non-OO - which might be OK for compiler
vendors'
> staff, but just doesn't make it easy for me to read as a reference
document.

in time

they then

version of
> OO, which ignoring Repository syntax, (which Pete just referred to),
doesn't make the
> early Micro Focus implementation dramatically out of step with the

>
> 1. "Elements of OO COBOL" - Wilson (Will) Price - available from
cobolreport.com. Both
> Pete and Don Tees have given this one a Gold Star. Tutorial in style,
starts with a
> conventional non-OO program and leads you gently from there.
>
> Will restarts each topic (chapter) picking up an additional aspect of OO -
there's
> even a brief coverage of DB and ESQL. He has done an excellent job. (It's
not actually
> in his book, but something he gave me separately as a white paper and
diskette source
> - the concept of COBOL file handling that I have turned into workable
code).
>
> I believe the old codger, (there's only a month or two difference in our
ages), still
> has it in him to write yet another book - before we both trot off into the
sunset.
> (Patience up THERE, I wasn't thinking of 'checking out' just yet).
>
> 2. "OO COBOL"  Edmund (Ed) C. Arranga and Frank P. Coyle. Available from
SIGS Books &
> Multimedia - SIGS - ISBN 1-884842-34-8 (pbk.) - http//www.sigs.com, or
Prentice Hall -
> ISBN 0-13-261140-6 . If you have trouble getting it - could always e-mail
Ed at
> cobolreport.com and see if he has a supply in his attic ! Note that Will
and Ed are

on the same
> wavelength.

> The Arranga/Coyle book does an excellent job. It's the first one I picked
up on and
> gave me my personal 'Epiphany' - a term I purloined from Ed because he
said exactly
> the same thing when the penny dropped for him as regards OOP. Not tutorial
like Will's

corporation for
> their own staff. Doesn't get into file handling but uses the DB as tables
in
> working-storage.

I take no issue with (in fact, endorse) Jimmy's recommendations above. But I
would add the following:

The "Fast Path" to understanding OO Concepts is via Java.

The COBOL implementation of OO (while extremely valuable) suffers from being
a "bolt on" to the COBOL Language. And, like all COBOL it is verbose. (This
is a two-edged sword and can be viewed as an advantage or a hindrance,
depending on your viewpoint and the requirements you measure it against.)

I spent a couple of months grappling with OO through the implementation of
OO COBOL and never really understood it. (OK, I may be slower than many of
you...<G>)

As soon as I picked up an introductory text on Java, the lights came on.

Because the language is DESIGNED around OO, the concepts of Classes and
Objects, Properties and Methods, are ingrained in it and seem perfectly
natural and logical. Also, there is more value to the Beginner in picking up
a "Teach Yourself" book which explains concepts and background rather than
trying to absorb this from the dry text of a Language spec.

So I learned Java (it took 4 weeks of daily study, up to 3 hours each day),
found it entertaining and useful (see the books I have recommended for this
under the "10 reasons why Java is better..." thread) and then re-approached
OO COBOL. Hey presto! No more problems with it... Added bonus, Java
capability... It can't be bad and I strongly recommend you all to do
likewise....<G>

It has also improved my use and understanding of the finer points of my
beloved PowerCOBOL...

>
> Simply put - OO syntax is not tremendously difficult to get a handle on.
What is
> EXTREMELY difficult is to comprehend how the concepts can literally be
turned on their
> heads, allowing you an amazing degree of flexibility in arriving at
business
> solutions, way beyond non-OO COBOL, although with some degree of effort
you can
> 'parallel' some aspects with structured.
>
I would contend that it isn't extremely difficult, it just requires a new
approach (I guess that IS extremely difficult for some people...<G>).
Newcomers to programming seem to have no problem with OO concepts.

In a business environment the OO concepts, by themselves, will not reap
tremendous advantages (although it is still preferable to traditional
programming methods). It requires new approaches  (which OO happens to be
ideal for..) and a movement away from the traditional Waterfall Management
of projects.

> A closing thought -  if this retiree can get a handle on it and use it,
sat here in
> splendid isolation, then for those with a wealth of COBOL  experience or
even the
> bright eyed, bushy tailed youngsters coming along, then they should be
able to do the
> same.
>
Yeah, if Jimmy can do it, ANYBODY can...<G>

Pete.
```

```
"Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> The COBOL implementation of OO (while extremely valuable) suffers from
being
> a "bolt on" to the COBOL Language. And, like all COBOL it is verbose.
(This
> is a two-edged sword and can be viewed as an advantage or a hindrance,
> depending on your viewpoint and the requirements you measure it against.)
________________

I hope you'll tolerate a tangent--I noticed the above in your remarks.

The verbosity = readability rubric has, since I first heard it, left me
skeptical.

I'll agree that the goal is enobled, but it doesn't pay out.  Are any
managers going to the code when they have a question?  Surely there would be
moments where it would be quicker to pop open a file than send an email and
wait for it to come back?

To be fair, a lot of programmers don't go to the code when THEY have a
question.  This isn't because of the code, it's because of the context.  To
properly understand any portion of a program you have to a grip on its place
in the global scheme.  It's this mass of background knowledge that ruins any
attempt to layman the code.

Perform this thought experiment:  Replace all of they keywords in Cobol with
appropriate 3-lettered abbreviations, given...

    (1)  No side effects are induced in mechanical
          interpretation of the code.

    (2)  New keywords that suggest their antecedants.
         (ie, don't change "EVALUATE" to "INV" and
          "INVOKE" to "EVL".)

    (3)  All printed and electronic references are
          also changed.

How long would it take for organizations to recover?

Would organizations completely recover?

The articulation and experience I've witnessed in this group suggests it
would be no more painful than 30 mph speed bump.
```

```
Grinder wrote:
```

```
Answer - not long at all, but of course M/F would be providing it to me as
'extensions'. Now as to when those extensions would become a  part of the
Standard ........?  Seriously - 10 years based on current 'alacrity'.

I deliberately chose COBOL because of it's verbosity - perhaps I am too
<G>. Now if I let micky-macks like you get away with this one, you'll then
suggest instead of those three character mnemonics let's substitute the
appropriate Assembler JMP, CMP or whatever. But that wont keep you happy -
next you will say, "Get rid of this crap" :-

*>-------------------------
Method-id. "do-somethng".
*>----------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-anynumber                        pic 9(03).
01 ls-anytext                              pic x(08).
Linkage section.
01 lnk-something                        object reference.
Procedure Division returning lnk-something.
    do some stuff
End Method  "do-somethng".
*>------------------------------

"And use this much more meaningful and terse function" :-

{dosomething
    {anynumber int
      anytext char

    {somethingout
    do something
}

OK I don't know the first thing about coding in hieroglyphics - but
there'll be no stopping you. Go play with VB or Java <G>

I can't recall whether it was you or Ross Klatte who made the comment -
but I have sat down with a user, an engineering application, and in the
days before sophisticated debugger Animators, read through my source code
in English, which he mentally took in, digested, and gave me the answer to
the problem.

Jimmy
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Verbosity

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-23T15:30:49-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9h2u9a$erl$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca> <3B339005.721A88B7@home.com> <3b33dfa9_5@Usenet.com> <4ZRY6.1023$PE2.70056@e420r-atl2.usenetserver.com> <3B340F36.586916A1@home.com> <EG4Z6.2189$f4.99277@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B34F0BF.B1EE6E7F@home.com> <4P6Z6.2813$f4.137934@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com>`

```
Just a personal opinion, but I agree that the "claim" that managers (or "end
users") can pick up some "real world" COBOL code and make sense out of it is
a "red herring".  It may or may not be true (certainly they MIGHT be able to
understand some of the "business logic" if data names and procedure names
are "good enough" - but I just do NOT see them ever wanting to do this.

HOWEVER,  I view the "readability" issues of COBOL (why it is a "strength")
as MUCH more to do with ANOTHER COBOL programmer being able to pick up
someone else's code and (relatively - very subjective) easily be able to
"understand it".

This is my problem with Tony (Foodman) Dilworth's approach (at least as I
"see it").  It is easy for him to maintain (enhance, test, whatever) his
code.  However, an "experienced" COBOL programmer would have a medium steep
learning curve to understand his "approach" (including structures and naming
conventions).

I come from an IBM mainframe background where there are LOTS of COBOL
programmers (sometimes multi-hundred) dealing with MANY different
applications (the majority in "maintenance" / "enhancement" mode).  It is
INCREDIBLY common for a programmer to move from project to project and
management wants them to "get up to speed" as quickly as possible.

Now we all (at least from my background) know about keeping "documentation"
current - you are SUPPOSED to do it, but programmers rarely do.  Therefore,
it is the "self-documenting" nature of COBOL
   FOR THE EXPERIENCED COBOL PROGRAMMER
that (to me) is its strength.
```

```
> "understand it".

Following through with the original experiment, though.  If all of the
keywords were suddenly abbreviated, wouldn't it just take a short time
for you to recover?

If EVALUATE were EVAL instead, would it be less readible to the
programmer?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Verbosity

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-23T17:29:37-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9h35a0$9id$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca> <3B339005.721A88B7@home.com> <3b33dfa9_5@Usenet.com> <4ZRY6.1023$PE2.70056@e420r-atl2.usenetserver.com> <3B340F36.586916A1@home.com> <EG4Z6.2189$f4.99277@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B34F0BF.B1EE6E7F@home.com> <4P6Z6.2813$f4.137934@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <9h2u9a$erl$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <bB8Z6.1011$pe.13321@e420r-atl3.usenetserver.com>`

```
A few things:

1) I don't see much difference in readability between "PIC" and "PICTURE" or
COMP and COMPUTATIONAL.  Therefore "reasonable" abbreviations certainly
would impact (IMHO) maintainability.

2) "Optional words" (such as "is" in  "picture IS") are certainly omitted my
many/most programmers (with minimal or no impact on maintainability).

3) I know of at least two existing compilers (Micro Focus/MERANT and IBM's
mainframe) where it is entirely POSSIBLE today to set up as many
"abbreviations" (alternate spellings) of COBOL keywords.   Although I have
seen some programmers (for medium-obscure reasons, changing COMP-5 to COMP),
I don't see there being any real demand for this functionality.  Therefore,
I do NOT think that using "abbreviations" of COBOL keywords would
significantly meet existing programmer needs/desires.  Furthermore, I do NOT
(personally) think that it is the "length" of the words that causes most
detractors to think of COBOL as "verbose".
```

```
Grinder wrote:
> 
> Following through with the original experiment, though.  If all of the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> programmer?

Is it that you think 'EVAL' is less 'verbose' read or to write ?

I have my editor set so that 2 keystrokes are all that is required for
most keywords.  It does not matter to my keying speed or volume whether
it is 'EVAL' or 'EVALUATE'.

When reading the source the actual size of the word makes little
difference to how my brain recognises it.  Actually it is more likely to
be able to readily distinquish SET, SORT, START, STRING and STOP rather
than STR, STP, SET, SRT, SRT.  (rearrange to correct order !).

'Verbosity' is not about number of characters, it is about the time
taken.  Reducing the characters does not reduce the time for _me_ to key
in, or for me to read and understand.

The answer to your question is 'Yes'.  EVAL _is_ less readable, or more
specifically 'EVL' (the 3 letters that you originally used) is because
it will initially be recognised (by me) as evil.  Even if I train my
mind to recognise EVL as EVALUATE then this does not reduce the _time_
taken to read the code, I still read it as the full word.

Encryption does not make it less 'verbose' only less understandable.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Verbosity

- **From:** "Grinder" <no.spam@no.spam.spam.spam.net>

```
I have focussed too much on this detail.  The larger question I would
like to capture, but find hard to distill into a sharp query, is if
you think that Cobol's verbosity is of any benefit.

It's hard to believe that it allows someone, who does not know Cobol
or anything about the system, to glean anything meaninful from the
code.  W. Klein points out that if not for the uninitiated, then for
other programmers.

If I try to home in on that, and try to improve my framing of
verbosity, it becomes much more difficult to construct an experiment.

The best example I can come up with is for mathematical formula.  Many
other languages tolerate a symbolic form of description where Cobol
opts for a more grammatical form.  Is this:

ADD 1 TO COUNT
MULTIPLY 2 BY COUNT

easier than:

Count = 2 * (Count + 1)

I have to admit, of course, that any precision test of this would
require an exhaustive, real world test.  Still, I don't think that my
speed or accuracy would be affected by having to adopt either syntax.

How about you?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Verbosity

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-06-24T17:44:52+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B361904.BFC14F3D@Azonic.co.nz>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca> <3B339005.721A88B7@home.com> <3b33dfa9_5@Usenet.com> <4ZRY6.1023$PE2.70056@e420r-atl2.usenetserver.com> <3B340F36.586916A1@home.com> <EG4Z6.2189$f4.99277@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B34F0BF.B1EE6E7F@home.com> <4P6Z6.2813$f4.137934@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <9h2u9a$erl$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <bB8Z6.1011$pe.13321@e420r-atl3.usenetserver.com> <3B35DAC8.D0034415@Azonic.co.nz> <VbdZ6.6285$f4.258647@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com>`

```
Grinder wrote:
> 
> I have focussed too much on this detail.  The larger question I would
> like to capture, but find hard to distill into a sharp query, is if
> you think that Cobol's verbosity is of any benefit.

You haven't yet approached the question: 'is Cobol Verbose' ?  merely
assuming that it is.  Making the keywords shorter does not make it less
verbose (in terms of reading _time_) for everyone.  An assembler
program, eg, may have a more concise syntax and less 'noise', but will
take more text to do the same task that a Cobol program will (in
general).
 
> It's hard to believe that it allows someone, who does not know Cobol
> or anything about the system, to glean anything meaninful from the
> code.  W. Klein points out that if not for the uninitiated, then for
> other programmers.

The original intent of the language was (IMHO) that it be readable by
such people as accountants and auditors.  By this means the mechanisms
in the code can be reviewed by people who understand the _system_
without needing to be overly concerned with (or confused by) the
semantics of the language.

In the 1950s Business Systems (non computer) were done by people with
their own language, the Business Systems design was expected to be
understood by those who had to implement it: accountants, scribes and
'computors' (people who computed, eg extended invoices, added up
ledgers).

By having Cobol readable by them too, they could confirm that the
computer system matched the Business System.

> If I try to home in on that, and try to improve my framing of
> verbosity, it becomes much more difficult to construct an experiment.
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Count = 2 * (Count + 1)

Cobol has 'tolerated' the symbolic form of COMPUTE Count = 2 * (Count +
1) for nearly 30 years.

If the reader comes to understand this by the process of: find the inner
expression; add 1 to count; multiply by 2; set into count.  Then it is
_no_less_verbose_, in fact maybe more.  Of course if the reader has
trained themselves in understanding the notation so they can grasp the
expression in one view then, yes, it is easier for _them_ to grasp.  Who
are your audience ?

For FORTRAN programs the audience was engineers and mathematicians who
were used to formulas, in fact FORTRAN is FORmula TRANslation: designed
to take their formulas directly into the source code.

It may well be that accountants are now used to spreadsheets and so will
understand such formulas as readily as the scripted method.  However
this does not make the formula less 'verbose' in the reading of it, only
that the reader needs to be trained in understanding infix notation
expressions so that they can be converted to 'verb form' for reading.

Anyway the introduction of COMPUTE may well reflect the change in the
audience as the Business People became used to formulas for their
business designs and to spreadsheets and similar tools.

BTW 'speadsheets' existed way before 'VisiCalc', originally as manual
calculation sheets then as electronic calculation mechanisms - for
example ICL's PROSPER in the 1960s and 70s.  Visicalc was the first
_visual_ electronic spreadsheet.
```

```
> You haven't yet approached the question: 'is Cobol Verbose' ?
merely
> assuming that it is.

This seems to be a commonly held perspective--Do you not rate Cobol
as a more verbose language than most other symbolic languages in use?

>                             Making the keywords shorter does not
make it less
> verbose (in terms of reading _time_) for everyone.

I'll concede this point--or at least that Cobol's alleged verbosity
has much
more to do with sequences of statements, rather than keyword size.

>
An assembler
> program, eg, may have a more concise syntax and less 'noise', but
will
> take more text to do the same task that a Cobol program will (in
> general).

Aren't concise and verbose antonyms?  The feature I'm trying to zoom
in on is the "wordiness" of the language--not the scope of it's atomic
statements.  Assembly has little latitude in defining it's syntax, as
it has
to have a tight mapping to processor instructions.

Higher level languages, such as Cobol and C++ have much more
flexibility
in defining it's statements verbosely or otherwise.

> > It's hard to believe that it allows someone, who does not know
Cobol
> > or anything about the system, to glean anything meaninful from the
> > code.  W. Klein points out that if not for the uninitiated, then
for
> > other programmers.
>
> The original intent of the language was (IMHO) that it be readable
by
> such people as accountants and auditors.  By this means the
mechanisms
> in the code can be reviewed by people who understand the _system_
> without needing to be overly concerned with (or confused by) the
> semantics of the language.

This is the benefit of Cobol's presumed verbosity that I'm interested
in--I
don't believe this has panned out for the reasons already stated.

> In the 1950s Business Systems (non computer) were done by people
with
> their own language, the Business Systems design was expected to be
> understood by those who had to implement it: accountants, scribes
and
> 'computors' (people who computed, eg extended invoices, added up
> ledgers).
>
> By having Cobol readable by them too, they could confirm that the
> computer system matched the Business System.

Has this benefit been realized?

> It may well be that accountants are now used to spreadsheets and so
will
> understand such formulas as readily as the scripted method.  However
> this does not make the formula less 'verbose' in the reading of it,
only
> that the reader needs to be trained in understanding infix notation
> expressions so that they can be converted to 'verb form' for
reading.

If spelling out each operation instead of assembling an infix notation
is not
a more verbose approach, what is?

> Anyway the introduction of COMPUTE may well reflect the change in
the
> audience as the Business People became used to formulas for their
> business designs and to spreadsheets and similar tools.

I would suggest that this is a reasonable expansion of the language,
in part
because it does not harm readability for anyone who will actually be
reading
the code.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Verbosity

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-06-25T08:55:51+01:00

```
Grinder wrote:
> 
> This seems to be a commonly held perspective--Do you not rate Cobol
> as a more verbose language than most other symbolic languages in use?

First the term 'verbose' needs to be defined in a useful way.  You seem
to be taking it entirely as the number of characters in the source
code.  

> >                             Making the keywords shorter does not
> make it less
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> more to do with sequences of statements, rather than keyword size.

And given that 'verbose' is 'wordiness' then reducing the size of a word
makes no difference at all to 'verbosity'.

> Aren't concise and verbose antonyms?  

It can have a more concise _syntax_ while the programs may be more
verbose (more total words).

>  The feature I'm trying to zoom
> in on is the "wordiness" of the language--not the scope of it's atomic
> statements.  Assembly has little latitude in defining it's syntax, as
> it has
> to have a tight mapping to processor instructions.

But to do a given task takes _more_ words.

> > The original intent of the language was (IMHO) that it be readable
> by
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> don't believe this has panned out for the reasons already stated.

Maybe, maybe not, but what is your point ?  You seem to want to change
Cobol.  If you don't like it as it currently is then just go and write C
or Java instead of trying to impose these on us by making Cobol into
being like them.

The alledged 'verbosity' of Cobol has never worried me, I use an editor
that writes the keywords with two keystrokes and reading formulas is no
less 'verbose', and anyway I use COMPUTE when appropriate.

> > By having Cobol readable by them too, they could confirm that the
> > computer system matched the Business System.
> 
> Has this benefit been realized?

Well I have had systems audited by business auditors (many years ago). 
They were not programmers or indeed anything to do with computers other
than using them.  They did actually read the parts of the program that
were concerned with pricing and discounting, and went away.


> If spelling out each operation instead of assembling an infix notation
> is not
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> the code.

COMPUTE is not 'an expansion of the language', it has been there for
nearly 30 years.  You seem not to know this.  Has your whole argument
been based on COMPUTE not existing for you ?
```

```
On Sun, 24 Jun 2001 14:32:01 -0500, "Grinder"
<no.spam@no.spam.spam.spam.net> wrote:

>> You haven't yet approached the question: 'is Cobol Verbose' ?
>merely
…[85 more quoted lines elided]…
>
I'm going out on a limb here - psychology is really not my field and I
invite anyone better qualified to address this hypothesis - 
Does not COBOL's "verbosity", or English language keywords, actually
hinder the interpretation of code?

In other words, given 2 equally competent programmers, one expert in a
symbolic language such as Assembler, the other in COBOL, wouldn't the
Assembly language programmer be more efficient?

My point is that the processing of language is a right-brain function.
Symbolic logic is processed as a left-brain function ( correct? )

Given that Assembly languages ( and, I believe, APL ) use symbols to
represent operations, wouldn't the process of coding in these
languages be more efficient that in COBOL; COBOL would require that a
right-brain function first interpret the words and convert them to
symbols for processing by the left-brain function?
It looks like a level an additional level of interpretation to me.

I suspect that COBOL programmers simply look at keywords, such as
EVALUATE, or even blocks of code, globally (as do speed readers) and
immediately recognise structures without interpreting details. At a
certain level of experience or competency, the keywords become
symbols, and structures are immediately recognisable without a
detailed reading.

This is much like what high-level chess players do - look at the board
and visualise strategies, without looking at the detailed movements of
individual pieces.

R. Miller
```

- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com> <3b2a3e70_3@Usenet.com> <3B2A4E60.5294064B@brazee.net> <3B328EF5.707E3593@istar.ca> <3B339005.721A88B7@home.com> <3b33dfa9_5@Usenet.com> <4ZRY6.1023$PE2.70056@e420r-atl2.usenetserver.com> <3B340F36.586916A1@home.com> <EG4Z6.2189$f4.99277@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <3B34F0BF.B1EE6E7F@home.com> <4P6Z6.2813$f4.137934@e420r-atl1.usenetserver.com> <9h2u9a$erl$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`

```


"William M. Klein" wrote:

> Just a personal opinion, but I agree that the "claim" that managers (or "end
> users") can pick up some "real world" COBOL code and make sense out of it is
…[87 more quoted lines elided]…
> >

Firstly a quick response to Grinder. Yes I could have given him the print-out of
the source. What actually happened was that I was reading the RM/COBOL source
from my screen, and he was looking over my shoulder <G>.

You are right on the button Bill  it is the SELF DOCUMENTING feature that is a
MAJOR, MAJOR plus for COBOL. And this is my objection to Tony's flowcharting or
any other flowcharting, including a written program specification produced by an
analyst. They get out of date and it is ONLY the program as coded that truly
represents how it functions within an application. (Apart from doing coding the
only other tool I still consider justifiable after some 40 years, is Decision
Tables).

Now when we move on to OO - and here I'm thinking of mainframes, big
installations - there truly are potential problems. Square one - serious thought
has to be given to the role of an OOA; same function for OO as a DBA for DBs. It
may well be that this is seen as a dual role with two lieutenants reporting to a
DB/OO 'leader'.

Assuming they adopt a three-tier design approach it does break the problem down
into units. Currently I have no idea on the consensus for OO coding style -
there just isn't even a 'minimum' to gauge against. I'll speculate as to Net
Express users - probably over 90% see OO as a tool to get at GUIs and the Net -
therefore they primarily code as structured, jumping off invoking GUI methods as
necessary. With the structured concept the OO-GUI-using application is not too
different from a real 'Structured system' - so for SELF DOCUMENTING - it follows
the same pattern.

Now adopt a three-tier approach :-

Business Class (the various programs 'called' from a menu). Just my style - but
in a similar manner to Stephen, I use a 'begin' method which is the controlling
'paragraph' to process the business logic  :-

        set ContinueProgram to true
         invoke self "do-step-1"

         if ContinueProgram
            invoke self "do-step-2"

            if etc....

Back to non-OO for a while. Possibly associated with segmentation we got into
the habit of using numbers to precede paragraph names, plus it allowed us to
break the problem down into discrete modules. Because of the ascending number
sequence fits fine with Self Documenting. Although paragraph numbering is still
widely used, there is a tendency for it to be dropped using only meaningful
paragraph names. What I hate more than anything is when I see :-

        Mainline.
        perform Open-files
        perform Get-Defaults
        perform Process-Input
        perform Print-It
        perform Any-Errors
        perform Close-files

        EXIT or Stop Run.

No problem with above - what I HATE is when the actual paragraphs are in that
EXACT same sequence. It would be no big deal to cut and paste to list the
paragraphs in ALPHABETICAL order - it is still Self Documenting and I'm not
flip-flopping between the equivalent of 1 to 50 pages of code trying to follow
the source.

Continuing with the Business Class - having extracted DBI(DataBase Interface)
and UIs(User Interface) as separate classes we need to know what they 'return'
to the Business Class. Again no problem with DBI because a DB access is invoked
and returns the result in the same piece of logic, so we aren't zipping though
the code looking fort alternatives.

GUIs are a bit different -  the Business class "creates", "shows" and waits for
events to be returned. The "creates" and "shows" are no big deal but the
'events' can pop up all over the place. Those events must be logically grouped
in some fashion so that the program maintainer can see where they occur, without
rummaging through the source  - boils down to an installation standard of how an
event receiving class should look. Fail to do this and you break the obvious
approach to Self Documenting.

DBIs and UIs - having been hived off as different tiers they are stand alone
modules accessed by Business classes. Most methods invoked are independently
invoked, (though some are PRIVATE to the invoked class and access to them is
triggered by the method you invoke from the Business class). As a group such
methods don't appear as one cohesive unit - therefore - again my style - I list
them in alphabetical sequence.

Providing mainframe users consider the above problems - arriving at their own
set of conclusions for readability - then Self Documentation still persists -
otherwise you finish up with a mish-mash - could turn out worse than Eileen's
fabled list of IF tests <G>

Jimmy
```

```
I leave PIC alone, but almost everywhere else, I use all of the
"optional" words, because they make it easier to understand at 2
o'clock in the morning. I always code IS EQUAL TO   or
STRING FIELD1
       SPACE
       FIELD2
       DELIMITED BY SIZE
           INTO FIELD3
           WITH POINTER FIELD4
               ON OVERFLOW ....etc
instead of
STRING FIELD1
       SPACE
       FIELD2
       DELIMITED SIZE
           INTO FIELD3
           POINTER FIELD4
               OVERFLOW ....etc

Those couple of little words make it so more readable..
Maintainability is a major goal.


Stephen J Spiro
Wizard Systems
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** thaneH@softwaresimple.com (Thane Hubbell)
- **Date:** 2001-06-16T18:41:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3b2b9af6.240407320@news1.attglobal.net>`
- **References:** `<9gbvri$v9e$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <20010615054623.11466.00003852@ng-mp1.aol.com>`

```
Ross - 

this. in Java is an object reference to "self".  All it does is ensure
that when you use a method or reference a property you get the one
associated with "this" object reference -- ensuring that it's not from
a superclass when the code you are executing at the time may actually
be in a superclass.

Consider this (This applies to OO COBOL as well ... substitute SELF
for "THIS").

ClassA
  Method1
  Method2
ClassB Inherits ClassA
  Method1 (override)
  Method2 (override)
  Method3

Now in method3 of class B, you invoke SUPER Method1 - that uses the
code that is in ClassA -- now in ClassA method 1 there is a "method2"
invocation.  You want to ensure that the method2 that is executed is
ALWAYS the  override (if there is one) -- so you use the "this" (COBOL
is SELF) object reference when specifying the method.


On 15 Jun 2001 09:46:23 GMT, klatteross@aol.commmm (Ross Klatte)
wrote:

>>From: "William M. Klein" wmklein@nospam.ix.netcom.com 
>>Date: 2001-06-14 23:36 Eastern Daylight Time
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

```
Multiple inheritance is extremely useful - and sadly missing in Java.
Do a search on Google for "Java Multiple Inheritence" and see all the
work arounds.  There are great reasons to use and have it.  One is for
basic things that you might not want to put in a BASE class becaue you
only need small pieces of it at a time.  These could be small
individual classes and you can inherit from many of these.
```

```
Yes please. Enlighten me. Seriously, I haven't yet figured out 'programming
wise' how I can inherit from both my maternal and paternal grandmothers. (Rick
Smith been slippin' you some info on the quiet. I recall he was thinking on this
one - quite a whiles back).

Are we back to invoking Up and Down again ?

So we are on the same wavelength, my specific query :-

Class-......................Class-...................Class-....................Class-

GrandMa-Walsh......GranDad-Walsh....GrandMa-Gavan....GranDad-Gavan

..............Class-MyMom...................................Class-MyDad

..............................................Class-Jimmy

So if in Class Jimmy I now have the following insruction :-
.
   invoke super "get-grannies-firstname" returning herName

How is it going to work its way through immediate supers MyMom and MyDad to hit
the appropriate 'super-super' Class-GrannieWalsh or Class-GrannieGavan ?
Even if I 'adjust' the method-name to help it in the search, it seems to me it
has got to 'trundle' up one branch of the hierachy arriving at a 'no go' and
then start all over again searching up through the other 'leg' of the hierachy.
(Actually it's even worse, it has to search the two Grannie classes, plus the
two GrandDad classes to confirm the method-name exists in one of those four
classes, unless you are real lucky and hit Class GrannieWalsh FIRST - and by
some magic, you knew that was the name I really wanted !).

But remember, it is supposed to be one of the real pluses of OO that I DON'T
have to adjust the method-name. A common method name is supposed to be reusable
through multiple objects.

Agreed, you can come up with 'work arounds' - but that is not multiple
inheritance.

Jimmy
```

```
"search" is
quite linear--look at the parents, then their parents, and so on.  It
comes at no
performance cost at runtime, because this resolution is done in a
pre-compile
operation.
```

```
Jon,

Go download the J4 document CD 1.10 and look at Annex  C, para C.17
onwards. Yes the Standard DOES allow for Multiple Inheritance, clearly
shows a Single Inheritance tree, but balks at showing an *actual* example
of Multiple Inheritance. It CAN be used but look at the caveats and syntax
included in the Standard to achieve it. (BTW - full marks to whoever on J4
was responsible for including the necessary syntax to achieve this, and at
the same time recognizing the problems).

Briefly it's there - in theory, which no doubt will also work - but at
this point in time, neither Fujitsu nor Net Express developers are able to
follow through on the theory and produce actual working code. It may well
be easily achievable,  might be resolved by a simpler approach, or might
just be an unnecessary mess.  The proof of the pudding is in the
eating........

Now taking my family tree example I think it is more tangible than Thane's
aircraft hierarchy - but I'll spell that out in more detail when I reply
to Thane. Now using true OO-ism I'm supposed to be able to have a common
method-name to use against 'like' classes, I could have said my message,
starting at a trigger program, initially invoking Jimmy and working up
through the tree, had the method-name "get-firstname" - just think of the
problems that is fraught with, without recognition of which specific
object your message is truly directed at.

At the moment for both Pete and myself, "The jury's still out". We at the
moment prefer a simpler approach. Show us hard coded evidence, compiled
and WORKS - then as appropriate, we will both possibly change our
point-of-view.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

- **From:** Joseph J Katnic <josephk@iinet.net.au>
- **Date:** 2001-06-17T21:20:31+08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3B2CAE9F.902@iinet.net.au>`
- **References:** `<20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com> <3b26c6b5_2@Usenet.com> <3b2b7439.230488627@news1.attglobal.net>`

```
Thane Hubbell wrote:

> 
> Multiple inheritance is extremely useful - and sadly missing in Java.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

Of course, If you want your class to contain some behaviour of another class, 
then there is nothing to stop you from defining an object of that class in your 
class and creating bridge methods to the included object.

My guess is that multiple inheritance is a shorthand way of achieving this 
without having to write all the bridging methods.

Unfortunately, debugging this can be a nightmare. If an object inherets methods 
from another class, how do you know that all of the inherited methods apply to 
the object. Some might be unappropriate. To ensure that only the appropriate 
methods are used, you have to override the inappropriate ones and null them out.

But, what if the inheretted class used it's methods on itself and you override 
one. Now the behaviour of the inheretted class is at risk because a method it 
requires has been overrided for a specific object instance.

It's just this undecidable nature of OO that worries me. How do I know that the 
method I use is in the current object/class or some other one? Which one is 
being used? How do I debug this mess?

I still personally believe that OO is a technique invented by the academics as 
an exercise in exclusion. Exclusion of the vast majority of practising 
programmers/system designers from the profession of teaching young programmers 
how to program.

Regards,
Joe Katnic.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Grinder" <no.spam@no.spam.spam.spam.net>
- **Date:** 2001-06-17T17:38:41-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<bgaX6.14753$RR.343727@e420r-atl2.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com> <3b26c6b5_2@Usenet.com> <3b2b7439.230488627@news1.attglobal.net> <3B2CAE9F.902@iinet.net.au>`

```
> > Multiple inheritance is extremely useful - and sadly missing in Java.
> > Do a search on Google for "Java Multiple Inheritence" and see all the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > individual classes and you can inherit from many of these.

> Of course, If you want your class to contain some behaviour of another
class,
> then there is nothing to stop you from defining an object of that class in
your
> class and creating bridge methods to the included object.
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> Unfortunately, debugging this can be a nightmare. If an object inherets
methods
> from another class, how do you know that all of the inherited methods
apply to
> the object. Some might be unappropriate. To ensure that only the
appropriate
> methods are used, you have to override the inappropriate ones and null
them out.

You can insure the inheritance of the proper set of methods by inheriting
from
the proper class.  Say you have a DataMatrix class that defines a CopyAll
method.  If
you want this capability for a UserNameList, but don't want to make it's
contents
copyable, you can structure a DataMatrixSecure class that's inherited by
DataMatrix
and UserNameList alike.  Most of the implementation of DataMatrix will go
into
DataMatrixSecure, but the CopyAll will remain.

> But, what if the inheretted class used it's methods on itself and you
override
> one. Now the behaviour of the inheretted class is at risk because a method
it
> requires has been overrided for a specific object instance.

I'm not sure what you mean here--how do you override a specific object
instance's methods--aren't we talking about class hierarchies?  Perhaps an
example would help me understand.

> It's just this undecidable nature of OO that worries me. How do I know
that the
> method I use is in the current object/class or some other one? Which one
is
> being used?

The method you're using is in the current class and each class up to the
base
definition of that method.  It's been my experience, though, that deep
hierarchies
of polymorphic methods are infrequent.  If a lot of this is going on, it's
probably
because there's a design problem that's torturing the implementation.
Indeed,
to implement like this is a misapplication of OOP principals.  (I've
honestly tried
to think of a scenario where this is warranted.  There probably is one, but
I
can't come up with one that can't be structured so as to avoid this.)

These scenarios are common:

1)  A base class defines an abstract method.  Inheritors of the class
implement
     this method polymorphically--the actual code is in the inheritor.

2)  A base class defines and implements a method.  Inheritors utilize this
without
     having to override the method, automatically.  The code is in the base
class.

> How do I debug this mess?

I'm honestly not being catty here--but it's the same for OOP and procedural
code.  A good interactive debugger and an understanding of the code that's
in the scope of your bug.

In either scenario, if the code is all spagged up, it will be harder, but I
believe
that OOP helps to structure code more effectively than structured
programming
conventions.  It's a lot like bee-keeping.  If you provide the swarm with a
comb
that has a regular hexagonal pattern to it, they will build a better
organized
system.  If not, they just pack it in wherever it fits.

> I still personally believe that OOP is a technique invented by the
academics as
> an exercise in exclusion. Exclusion of the vast majority of practising
> programmers/system designers from the profession of teaching young
programmers
> how to program.

Seriously?  How is the exclusion being perpetrated?  There are skads of
tutorials,
code bases and general discussions of OOP available.  What's to stop anyone
from
from learning this?

Everyone has a different aesthetic.  If you don't like OOP, that's tenable,
but it doesn't
make it a bad idea.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Editor" <Editor@aboutlegacycoding.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-18T10:46:42-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gl487$ott$1@allthetime.news.cais.net>`
- **References:** `<sUSV6.21342$JS1.1187767@bin2.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <20010613195619.04650.00000284@ng-ba1.aol.com> <RT2W6.28799$JS1.1721545@bin2.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <9gar20$pms$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net> <3b295d3d_3@Usenet.com>`

```
Pete,
It's not a matter of "echoing around the world" and don't assume that we in
N/A have any special privlidges.

NTTP protocol, like HTML protocol is not "guaranteed delivery."  With HTML,
if the net is extremely busy, or you are down a poor connection, or some
jerk with a backhoe dug up part of the backbone, then you get 404 errors
(but when you hit refresh you get the page), or you get broken graphics,
etc.

With news messages (NTTP protocol), as messages are relayed between servers,
if the net is slow or a packet gets damaged, etc. the message simply doesn't
get through.

The quality of a news server (i.e. how many messages you see) is sometimes a
function of how close to the backbone and how good their peering connections
are.  Up until last month I was on a news server on PSI (who recently went
Bankrupt).  I was lucky if I saw 10% of the messages in the group.  I know
this because I was always seeing missing messages echoed in replies.  More
than once I've e-mailed one of you to ask if you had a copy of something I
missed that I wanted to see (and thank the ones who helped by replying!).

Now I'm on a news server at CAIS Internet and I'm seeing about 90% of the
messages.  That's just life on the Internet.

I've never been on a news server that I felt gave me 100%, but some of the
web based systems are better.  For example http://www.etin.com/ I frequently
go there when I think I've missed something.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Editor" <Editor@aboutlegacycoding.com>
- **Date:** 2001-06-19T11:15:51-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gnqau$30p4$1@allday.news.cais.net>`
- **References:** `<sUSV6.21342$JS1.1187767@bin2.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <20010613195619.04650.00000284@ng-ba1.aol.com> <RT2W6.28799$JS1.1721545@bin2.nnrp.aus1.giganews.com> <9gar20$pms$1@slb7.atl.mindspring.net> <3b295d3d_3@Usenet.com> <9gl487$ott$1@allthetime.news.cais.net> <rXAX6.87537$4f7.7100565@bgtnsc06-news.ops.worldnet.att.net>`

```
Russell,
I'm sure that the .JPG's that a fine upstanding citizen like you were
looking at were on the alt.fan.johnwayne newsgroup right? *grin*

I don't think it's a matter of different newsgroups so much as the content
of that news group.  Administrators can set the number or days or the number
of bytes of stuff that a news server holds.  And they can set this by group.
So many of them will hold 7 or 10 days of stuff in mostly text based groups
but only 3 days in the binary groups.

However, the larger problem is file size.  A text only message like this one
only takes a few k.  A binary like a jpg takes 20- 100k or more.  So the
chance that a bad packet or dropped line will interrupt a large jpg is many
times greater than for a text message.
```

#### ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

- **From:** kanoako@netnet.net (Kano Ako)

```
klatteross@aol.commmm (Ross Klatte) wrote in message news:<20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com>...
> 1.  JAVA can read a string as an integer with the elegant
>     int iVar=Integer.valueOf(stringVar).valueInt;
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> B-o-r-i-n-g.  

Costs you a function call.


> 
> 2.  JAVA has a wide assortment of methods to read input data--InputStream,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> B-o-rrr-inggg. 

I have not used a cobol read in a long time or can I even remember the
last time I saw one.


> 
> 3.  JAVA reads data the way it is supposed to be read--one byte at a time. 
>     cobol lumps everything into one monolithic monstrosity called a "record." 
> Boring.  Boring and primitive.  

Wrong!  Byte by byte reading is primitive, record reading is more
natural.

It is more common that applications require groups of bytes rather
than single byte by single byte.  So with byte by byte reading one
must collect bytes  until the information unit is completely gathered.
 In fact I consider the innate ability of COBOL to define, redefine
and breakdown records to be its strongest feature!

Anyway I recognize you as a troll since your standard of a good
language seems to be that which is not boring vs that which is boring.

Anyway
KA
```

```
Uhh in my work the preferred method of writing and reading sequential
```

```
The "exclusive" use of GSAM (even in an IMS-only shop) to the exclusion of
QSAM and VSAM (which implement "standard" COBOL I/O) is unusual - from my
experience.  I suppose it COULD be done as a "shop standard" - or at least
for one application, but I (personally) have never heard of this actually
happened before.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Peter E. C. Dashwood" <dashwood@nospam.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2001-06-21T02:21:08+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3b30b18a_3@Usenet.com>`
- **References:** `<20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com> <677e6c81.0106151309.100dbf8e@posting.google.com> <3B2A9614.57CAA129@home.com> <677e6c81.0106181448.2cad2151@posting.google.com> <3B2E99E1.42DD5589@home.com> <9go2b6$lok$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3b301fea.82106209@news1.attglobal.net>`

```
When IMS first started making its presence felt (early to mid-70s) it was
common practice to define existing VSAM datasets as GSAM databases so they
could be accessed hierarchically sequentially with IMS.

It was intended as a "conversion" aid and NOBODY expected the GSAM databases
to continue, once applications were converted to "full" IMS.

We invented our own mnemonics for these things...These were dutifully
expanded with a straight face to new trainees... here are the ones I
remember (and that are printable...)

BTAM =  Bloody Terrible Access Method
QTAM =  Quietly Treacherous Access Method
VTAM =  Very Tricky Access Method
TCAM =  The Cat's Attacking Me
BDAM = Bloody Dangerous Access Method
QDAM = Quite Disastrous Access Method
(Logic tells me there MUST have been a VDAM, but I don't remember using it.
I have used and/or programmed all the others in this list...)
BSAM = Blatantly Silly Access Method
QSAM = Quite Silly Access Method
VSAM = Very Silly Access Method
  ISAM =  Incredibly Silly Access Method
GSAM =  Goddamned Stupid Access Method
 KSDS=  Keep Still Don't Struggle
 ESDS=  Extremely Stupid Data Storage
 RRDS=  Relatively Risky Data Shredding

(Makes PC ISAM look a bit anaemic, doesn't it...<G> Guess that's why I moved
to RDB...)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: 10 Reasons Why JAVA is better than cobol

- **From:** chammond@lc.cc.il.us
- **Date:** 2001-06-20T20:09:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gqvtg$q2o$1@news.netmar.com>`

```
In article <20010612190911.12598.00000033@ng-mh1.aol.com>, Ross Klatte
<klatteross@aol.commmm> writes:
>1.  JAVA can read a string as an integer with the elegant
>    int iVar=Integer.valueOf(stringVar).valueInt;
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>    cobol lumps everything into one monolithic monstrosity called a "record."

>Boring.  Boring and primitive.  
>
>   
Java reads data however the file reader you choose reads the file.  In java
you can choose to read one byte at a time if you choose, however that doesnt
do you much good when the data is in text.  Java can read text one character
at a time also.  Then when you have data that is comma separated you may be
better off reading one line at a time if it is a file with the carriage
returns in the  file.  For this you need to read one line at a time.  Of
course you left off buffering.  If you use a BufferedReader you can read data
faster than one byte at a time.  You can also read data one Byte Array at a
time which is even faster in Java.  Reading data one byte at a time is very
innefficient and wastes processing Cycles.  Only an rank Newbee Java
Programmer who wants to slow down their computer would read a file in this
manner. 
>Ross
>http://www.geocities.com/ross_klatte/
>
>
```
